<p><b>The Goldman Sachs Group, Inc.</b> is an American <a href="/wiki/Multinational_corporation" title="Multinational corporation">multinational</a> <a href="/wiki/Financial_services" title="Financial services">finance company</a> that engages in global <a href="/wiki/Investment_banking" title="Investment banking">investment banking</a>, <a href="/wiki/Investment_management" title="Investment management">investment management</a>, <a href="/wiki/Security_(finance)" title="Security (finance)">securities</a>, and other <a href="/wiki/Financial_services" title="Financial services">financial services</a>, primarily with institutional clients.</p>
<p>Goldman Sachs was founded in 1869 and is headquartered at <a href="/wiki/200_West_Street" title="200 West Street">200 West Street</a> in <a href="/wiki/Lower_Manhattan" title="Lower Manhattan">Lower Manhattan</a>, New York City, with additional offices in other international financial centers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup> The firm provides <a href="/wiki/Asset_management" title="Asset management">asset management</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mergers_and_acquisitions" title="Mergers and acquisitions">mergers and acquisitions</a> advice, <a href="/wiki/Prime_brokerage" title="Prime brokerage">prime brokerage</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Securities_underwriting" title="Securities underwriting">underwriting services</a> to its clients, which include corporations, governments, and individuals. The firm also engages in <a href="/wiki/Market_maker" title="Market maker">market making</a> and <a href="/wiki/Private_equity" title="Private equity">private equity</a> deals, and is a <a href="/wiki/Primary_dealer" title="Primary dealer">primary dealer</a> in the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Treasury_security" title="United States Treasury security">U.S. Treasury security</a> market.</p>
<p>Due to its involvement in <a href="/wiki/Subprime_mortgage_crisis" title="Subprime mortgage crisis">subprime mortgages</a>, Goldman Sachs was hit hard by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/2008_economic_crisis" title="2008 economic crisis">2008 economic crisis</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup> and was subsequently rescued as part of a massive <a href="/wiki/Emergency_Economic_Stabilization_Act_of_2008" title="Emergency Economic Stabilization Act of 2008">U.S. government bailout</a>.</p>
<p><a href="/wiki/List_of_former_employees_of_Goldman_Sachs" title="List of former employees of Goldman Sachs">Former Goldman executives</a> who moved on to government positions include former <a href="/wiki/United_States_Secretary_of_the_Treasury" title="United States Secretary of the Treasury">U.S. Secretaries of the Treasury</a> <a href="/wiki/Robert_Rubin" title="Robert Rubin">Robert Rubin</a> and <a href="/wiki/Henry_Paulson" title="Henry Paulson">Henry Paulson</a>; <a href="/wiki/European_Central_Bank" title="European Central Bank">European Central Bank</a> President <a href="/wiki/Mario_Draghi" title="Mario Draghi">Mario Draghi</a>; former <a href="/wiki/Bank_of_Canada" title="Bank of Canada">Bank of Canada</a> Governor and current Governor of the <a href="/wiki/Bank_of_England" title="Bank of England">Bank of England</a> <a href="/wiki/Mark_Carney" title="Mark Carney">Mark Carney</a> and the current <a href="/wiki/Prime_Minister_of_Australia" title="Prime Minister of Australia">Prime Minister of Australia</a> <a href="/wiki/Malcolm_Turnbull" title="Malcolm Turnbull">Malcolm Turnbull</a>. Second-generation Goldman alum <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Steve_Mnuchin" title="Steve Mnuchin">Steve Mnuchin</a> has been nominated by <a href="/wiki/Donald_Trump" title="Donald Trump">Donald Trump</a> to serve as <a href="/wiki/United_States_Secretary_of_the_Treasury" title="United States Secretary of the Treasury">United States Secretary of the Treasury</a> in his administration.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup></p>
<p></p>

<p></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Goldman_Sachs&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="1869.E2.80.931930">1869–1930</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Goldman_Sachs&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: 1869–1930">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Goldman%E2%80%93Sachs_family" title="Goldman–Sachs family">Goldman–Sachs family</a></div>
<p>Goldman Sachs was founded in <a href="/wiki/New_York_City" title="New York City">New York</a> in 1869 by <a href="/wiki/Marcus_Goldman" title="Marcus Goldman">Marcus Goldman</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mgoldman_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-mgoldman-7">[7]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup> In 1882, Goldman's son-in-law <a href="/wiki/Samuel_Sachs" title="Samuel Sachs">Samuel Sachs</a> joined the firm.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup> In 1885, Goldman took his son Henry and his son-in-law Ludwig Dreyfuss into the business and the firm adopted its present name, Goldman Sachs &amp; Co.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup> The company made a name for itself pioneering the use of <a href="/wiki/Commercial_paper" title="Commercial paper">commercial paper</a> for entrepreneurs and joined the <a href="/wiki/New_York_Stock_Exchange" title="New York Stock Exchange">New York Stock Exchange</a> (NYSE) in 1896.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cohan2012_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-Cohan2012-11">[11]</a></sup> By 1898, the firm's capital stood at $1.6 million (about $46 million in <a href="/wiki/Real_versus_nominal_value_(economics)" title="Real versus nominal value (economics)">real</a> value), and was growing rapidly.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cohan2012_11-1"><a href="#cite_note-Cohan2012-11">[11]</a></sup></p>
<p>Goldman entered the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/IPO" title="IPO">IPO</a> market in 1906 when it took <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sears,_Roebuck_and_Company" title="Sears, Roebuck and Company">Sears, Roebuck and Company</a> public.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cohan2012_11-2"><a href="#cite_note-Cohan2012-11">[11]</a></sup> The deal occurred due to Henry Goldman's personal friendship with the owner of Sears, <a href="/wiki/Julius_Rosenwald" title="Julius Rosenwald">Julius Rosenwald</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cohan2012_11-3"><a href="#cite_note-Cohan2012-11">[11]</a></sup> Other IPOs followed, including <a href="/wiki/F._W._Woolworth_Company" title="F. W. Woolworth Company">F. W. Woolworth</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Continental_Can" title="Continental Can">Continental Can</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cohan2012_11-4"><a href="#cite_note-Cohan2012-11">[11]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 1912, Henry S. Bowers became the first non-family member and non-Jew to become partner.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cohan2012_11-5"><a href="#cite_note-Cohan2012-11">[11]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 1917, under growing pressure from the other partners in the firm due to his pro-German stance, Henry Goldman resigned.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cohan2012_11-6"><a href="#cite_note-Cohan2012-11">[11]</a></sup> Control of the firm was now in the hands of the Sachs family.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cohan2012_11-7"><a href="#cite_note-Cohan2012-11">[11]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Waddill_Catchings" title="Waddill Catchings">Waddill Catchings</a> joined the company in 1918.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cohan2012_11-8"><a href="#cite_note-Cohan2012-11">[11]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 1920, the firm moved from 60 <a href="/wiki/Wall_Street" title="Wall Street">Wall Street</a> to $1.5 million 12-story premises on 30–32 Pine Street.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cohan2012_11-9"><a href="#cite_note-Cohan2012-11">[11]</a></sup></p>
<p>By 1928, Catchings was the Goldman partner with the single largest stake in the firm.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cohan2012_11-10"><a href="#cite_note-Cohan2012-11">[11]</a></sup></p>
<p>On December 4, 1928, the firm launched the Goldman Sachs Trading Corp., a <a href="/wiki/Closed-end_fund" title="Closed-end fund">closed-end fund</a>. The fund failed during the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Stock_Market_Crash_of_1929" title="Stock Market Crash of 1929">Stock Market Crash of 1929</a>, amid accusations that Goldman had engaged in share price manipulation and <a href="/wiki/Insider_trading" title="Insider trading">insider trading</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cohan2012_11-11"><a href="#cite_note-Cohan2012-11">[11]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="1930.E2.80.931980">1930–1980</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Goldman_Sachs&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: 1930–1980">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In 1930, the firm ousted Catchings, and <a href="/wiki/Sidney_Weinberg" title="Sidney Weinberg">Sidney Weinberg</a> assumed the role of senior partner and shifted Goldman's focus away from trading and toward <a href="/wiki/Investment_banking" title="Investment banking">investment banking</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cohan2012_11-12"><a href="#cite_note-Cohan2012-11">[11]</a></sup> It was Weinberg's actions that helped to restore some of Goldman's tarnished reputation. On the back of Weinberg, Goldman was lead advisor on the <a href="/wiki/Ford_Motor_Company" title="Ford Motor Company">Ford Motor Company</a>'s IPO in 1956, which at the time was a major coup on Wall Street. Under Weinberg's reign the firm also started an investment research division and a <a href="/wiki/Municipal_bond" title="Municipal bond">municipal bond</a> department. It also was at this time that the firm became an early innovator in <a href="/wiki/Risk_arbitrage" title="Risk arbitrage">risk arbitrage</a>.</p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Gus_Levy" title="Gus Levy">Gus Levy</a> joined the firm in the 1950s as a securities trader, which started a trend at Goldman where there would be two powers generally vying for supremacy, one from investment banking and one from securities trading. For most of the 1950s and 1960s, this would be Weinberg and Levy. Levy was a pioneer in <a href="/wiki/Block_trade" title="Block trade">block trading</a> and the firm established this trend under his guidance. Due to Weinberg's heavy influence at the firm, it formed an investment banking division in 1956 in an attempt to spread around influence and not focus it all on Weinberg.</p>
<p>In 1969, Levy took over as Senior Partner from Weinberg, and built Goldman's trading franchise once again. It is Levy who is credited with Goldman's famous philosophy of being "long-term greedy", which implied that as long as money is made over the long term, trading losses in the short term were not to be worried about. At the same time, partners reinvested almost all of their earnings in the firm, so the focus was always on the future.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup> That same year, Weinberg retired from the firm.</p>
<p>Another financial crisis for the firm occurred in 1970, when the <a href="/wiki/Penn_Central_Transportation_Company" title="Penn Central Transportation Company">Penn Central Transportation Company</a> went bankrupt with over $80 million in <a href="/wiki/Commercial_paper" title="Commercial paper">commercial paper</a> outstanding, most of it issued through Goldman Sachs. The bankruptcy was large, and the resulting lawsuits, notably by the <a href="/wiki/U.S._Securities_and_Exchange_Commission" title="U.S. Securities and Exchange Commission">SEC</a>, threatened the partnership capital, life and reputation of the firm.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cohan_wpost2012_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-cohan_wpost2012-13">[13]</a></sup> It was this bankruptcy that resulted in <a href="/wiki/Credit_rating" title="Credit rating">credit ratings</a> being created for every issuer of commercial paper today by several credit rating services.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hahn_Instruments_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hahn_Instruments-14">[14]</a></sup></p>
<p>During the 1970s, the firm also expanded in several ways. Under the direction of Senior Partner Stanley R. Miller, it opened its first international office in London in 1970, and created a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Private_Wealth_Management" title="Private Wealth Management">private wealth</a> division along with a <a href="/wiki/Fixed_income" title="Fixed income">fixed income</a> division in 1972. It also pioneered the "<a href="/wiki/White_knight_(business)" title="White knight (business)">white knight</a>" strategy in 1974 during its attempts to defend Electric Storage Battery against a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hostile_takeover" title="Hostile takeover">hostile takeover</a> bid from International Nickel and Goldman's rival <a href="/wiki/Morgan_Stanley" title="Morgan Stanley">Morgan Stanley</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bloomberg_2004_Friedman_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bloomberg_2004_Friedman-15">[15]</a></sup> This action would boost the firm's reputation as an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Investment_advisor" title="Investment advisor">investment advisor</a> because it pledged to no longer participate in hostile takeovers.</p>
<p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/John_Weinberg" title="John Weinberg">John L. Weinberg</a> (the son of Sidney Weinberg), and <a href="/wiki/John_C._Whitehead" title="John C. Whitehead">John C. Whitehead</a> assumed roles of co-senior partners in 1976, once again emphasizing the co-leadership at the firm. One of their initiatives<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup> was the establishment of 14 business principles that the firm still claims to apply.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Goldman_Sachs_14_principles_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-Goldman_Sachs_14_principles-17">[17]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="1980.E2.80.931999">1980–1999</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Goldman_Sachs&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: 1980–1999">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>On November 16, 1981, the firm acquired J. Aron &amp; Company, a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Commodities_trading" title="Commodities trading">commodities trading</a> firm which merged with the Fixed Income division to become known as Fixed Income, Currencies, and Commodities. J. Aron was a player in the coffee and gold markets, and the current CEO of Goldman, <a href="/wiki/Lloyd_Blankfein" title="Lloyd Blankfein">Lloyd Blankfein</a>, joined the firm as a result of this merger. In 1985 it underwrote the public offering of the <a href="/wiki/Real_estate_investment_trust" title="Real estate investment trust">real estate investment trust</a> that owned <a href="/wiki/Rockefeller_Center" title="Rockefeller Center">Rockefeller Center</a>, then the largest <a href="/wiki/Real_estate_investment_trust" title="Real estate investment trust">REIT</a> offering in history. In accordance with the beginning of the <a href="/wiki/Dissolution_of_the_Soviet_Union" title="Dissolution of the Soviet Union">dissolution of the Soviet Union</a>, the firm also became involved in facilitating the global privatization movement by advising companies that were spinning off from their parent governments.</p>
<p>In 1986, the firm formed Goldman Sachs Asset Management, which manages the majority of its mutual funds and <a href="/wiki/Hedge_fund" title="Hedge fund">hedge funds</a> today. In the same year, the firm also underwrote the IPO of <a href="/wiki/Microsoft" title="Microsoft">Microsoft</a>, advised <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/GE" title="GE">General Electric</a> on its acquisition of <a href="/wiki/RCA" title="RCA">RCA</a> and joined the <a href="/wiki/London_Stock_Exchange" title="London Stock Exchange">London</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tokyo_stock_exchange" title="Tokyo stock exchange">Tokyo stock exchanges</a>. 1986 also was the year when Goldman became the first United States bank to rank in the top 10 of <a href="/wiki/Mergers_and_acquisitions" title="Mergers and acquisitions">mergers and acquisitions</a> in the United Kingdom. During the 1980s the firm became the first bank to distribute its investment research electronically and created the first public offering of original issue deep-discount <a href="/wiki/Bond_(finance)" title="Bond (finance)">bond</a>.</p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Robert_Rubin" title="Robert Rubin">Robert Rubin</a> and <a href="/wiki/Stephen_Friedman_(PFIAB)" title="Stephen Friedman (PFIAB)">Stephen Friedman</a> assumed the Co-Senior Partnership in 1990 and pledged to focus on globalization of the firm and strengthening the Merger &amp; Acquisition and Trading business lines. During their reign, the firm introduced paperless trading to the New York Stock Exchange and lead-managed the first-ever global <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sub-sovereign_bonds" title="Sub-sovereign bonds">debt offering</a> by a U.S. corporation. It also launched the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Goldman_Sachs_Commodity_Index" title="Goldman Sachs Commodity Index">Goldman Sachs Commodity Index</a> (GSCI) and opened a Beijing office in 1994.</p>
<p>Also in 1994, <a href="/wiki/Jon_Corzine" title="Jon Corzine">Jon Corzine</a> assumed leadership of the firm as CEO, following the departure of Rubin and Friedman.</p>
<p>Another momentous event in Goldman's history was the Mexican bailout of 1995. Rubin drew criticism in Congress for using a Treasury Department account under his personal control to distribute $20 billion to bail out Mexican bonds, of which Goldman was a key distributor.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup> On November 22, 1994, the Mexican Bolsa stock market had admitted Goldman Sachs and one other firm to operate on that market.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup> The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/1994_economic_crisis_in_Mexico" title="1994 economic crisis in Mexico">1994 economic crisis in Mexico</a> threatened to wipe out the value of Mexico's bonds held by Goldman Sachs.</p>
<p>The firm joined <a href="/wiki/David_Rockefeller" title="David Rockefeller">David Rockefeller</a> and partners in a 50–50 joint ownership of <a href="/wiki/Rockefeller_Center" title="Rockefeller Center">Rockefeller Center</a> during 1994, but later sold the shares to <a href="/wiki/Tishman_Speyer" title="Tishman Speyer">Tishman Speyer</a> in 2000. In 1996, Goldman was lead underwriter of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Yahoo" title="Yahoo">Yahoo</a>! IPO and in 1998 it was global coordinator of the <a href="/wiki/NTT_DoCoMo" title="NTT DoCoMo">NTT DoCoMo</a> IPO.</p>
<p>In 1999, Henry Paulson took over as Senior Partner.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Initial_public_offering_in_1999">Initial public offering in 1999</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Goldman_Sachs&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Initial public offering in 1999">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>One of the largest events in the firm's history was its own <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Initial_Public_Offering" title="Initial Public Offering">IPO</a> in 1999. The decision to go public was one that the partners debated for decades. In the end, Goldman decided to offer only a small portion of the company to the public, with some 48% still held by the partnership pool.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup> 22% of the company was held by non-partner employees, and 18% was held by retired Goldman partners and two longtime investors, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sumitomo_Bank_Ltd." title="Sumitomo Bank Ltd.">Sumitomo Bank Ltd.</a> and Hawaii's Kamehameha Activities Assn (the investing arm of <a href="/wiki/Kamehameha_Schools" title="Kamehameha Schools">Kamehameha Schools</a>). This left approximately 12% of the company as being held by the public. With the firm's 1999 IPO, Paulson became Chairman and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chief_Executive_Officer" title="Chief Executive Officer">CEO</a> of the firm.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Acquisitions_.281999-2009.29">Acquisitions (1999-2009)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Goldman_Sachs&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Acquisitions (1999-2009)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In 1999, Goldman acquired <a href="/wiki/Hull_Trading_Company" title="Hull Trading Company">Hull Trading Company</a>, one of the world's premier market-making firms, for $531 million. More recently, the firm has been busy both in <a href="/wiki/Investment_banking" title="Investment banking">investment banking</a> and in trading activities. It purchased Spear, Leeds, &amp; Kellogg, one of the largest specialist firms on the New York Stock Exchange, for $6.3 billion in September 2000. It also advised on a debt offering for the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Government_of_the_People%27s_Republic_of_China" title="Government of the People's Republic of China">Government of China</a> and the first electronic offering for the <a href="/wiki/World_Bank" title="World Bank">World Bank</a>. In 2003 it took a 45% stake in a joint venture with JBWere, the Australian investment bank. In 2009, the Private Wealth Management arm of JBWere was sold into a joint venture with National Australia Bank. Goldman opened a full-service broker-dealer in <a href="/wiki/Brazil" title="Brazil">Brazil</a> in 2007, after having set up an investment banking office in 1996. It expanded its investments in companies to include <a href="/wiki/Burger_King" title="Burger King">Burger King</a>, McJunkin Corporation,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup> and in January 2007, <a href="/wiki/Alliance_Atlantis" title="Alliance Atlantis">Alliance Atlantis</a> alongside <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/CanWest_Global_Communications" title="CanWest Global Communications">CanWest Global Communications</a> to own sole broadcast rights to the <a href="/wiki/CSI_(franchise)" title="CSI (franchise)">all three <i>CSI</i> series</a>.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Change_in_leadership_.282006.29">Change in leadership (2006)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Goldman_Sachs&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Change in leadership (2006)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In May 2006, Paulson left the firm to serve as <a href="/wiki/United_States_Secretary_of_the_Treasury" title="United States Secretary of the Treasury">United States Secretary of the Treasury</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lloyd_C._Blankfein" title="Lloyd C. Blankfein">Lloyd C. Blankfein</a> was promoted to Chairman and Chief Executive Officer. Former Goldman employees have headed the New York Stock Exchange, the World Bank, the U.S. Treasury Department, the White House staff, and firms such as <a href="/wiki/Citigroup" title="Citigroup">Citigroup</a> and <a href="/wiki/Merrill_Lynch" title="Merrill Lynch">Merrill Lynch</a>.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Actions_in_the_2007.E2.80.932008_mortgage_crisis">Actions in the 2007–2008 mortgage crisis</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Goldman_Sachs&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Actions in the 2007–2008 mortgage crisis">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>During the <a href="/wiki/Subprime_mortgage_crisis" title="Subprime mortgage crisis">2007 subprime mortgage crisis</a>, Goldman was able to profit from the collapse in subprime mortgage bonds in the summer of 2007 by <a href="/wiki/Short_(finance)" title="Short (finance)">short-selling</a> subprime <a href="/wiki/Mortgage-backed_security" title="Mortgage-backed security">mortgage-backed securities</a>. Two Goldman traders, Michael Swenson and Josh Birnbaum, are credited with being responsible for the firm's large profits during the crisis.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup> The pair, members of Goldman's <a href="/wiki/Structured_product" title="Structured product">structured products</a> group in <a href="/wiki/New_York_City" title="New York City">New York</a>, made a profit of $4 billion by "betting" on a collapse in the sub-prime market, and shorting mortgage-related securities. By summer 2007, they persuaded colleagues to see their point of view and convinced skeptical risk management executives.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated1_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated1-24">[24]</a></sup> The firm initially avoided large subprime writedowns, and achieved a net profit due to significant losses on non-prime <a href="/wiki/Securitization" title="Securitization">securitized</a> loans being offset by gains on short mortgage positions. The firm's viability was later called into question as the crisis intensified in September 2008.</p>
<p>On October 15, 2007, as the crisis had begun to unravel, <a href="/wiki/Allan_Sloan" title="Allan Sloan">Allan Sloan</a>, a senior editor for <i><a href="/wiki/Fortune_(magazine)" title="Fortune (magazine)">Fortune</a></i> magazine, said:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup></p>
<blockquote>
<p>So let's reduce this macro story to human scale. Meet GSAMP Trust 2006-S3, a $494 million drop in the junk-mortgage bucket, part of the more than half-a-trillion dollars of mortgage-backed securities issued last year. We found this issue by asking mortgage mavens to pick the worst deal they knew of that had been floated by a top-tier firm – and this one's pretty bad.</p>
<p>It was sold by Goldman Sachs – GSAMP originally stood for Goldman Sachs Alternative Mortgage Products but now has become a name itself, like <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/AT%26T_Inc." title="AT&amp;T Inc.">AT&amp;T</a> and <a href="/wiki/3M" title="3M">3M</a>.</p>
<p>This issue, which is backed by ultra-risky second-mortgage loans, contains all the elements that facilitated the housing bubble and bust. It's got speculators searching for quick gains in hot housing markets; it's got loans that seem to have been made with little or no serious analysis by lenders; and finally, it's got Wall Street, which churned out mortgage "product" because buyers wanted it. As they say on the Street, "When the ducks quack, feed them."</p>
</blockquote>
<p>On September 21, 2008, Goldman Sachs and Morgan Stanley, the last two major investment banks in the United States, both confirmed that they would become traditional <a href="/wiki/Bank_holding_company" title="Bank holding company">bank holding companies</a>, bringing an end to the era of investment banking on <a href="/wiki/Wall_Street" title="Wall Street">Wall Street</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup> The Federal Reserve's approval of their bid to become banks ended the ascendancy of the securities firms, 75 years after Congress separated them from deposit-taking lenders, and capped weeks of chaos that sent <a href="/wiki/Lehman_Brothers" title="Lehman Brothers">Lehman Brothers</a> into bankruptcy and led to the rushed sale of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Merrill_Lynch_%26_Co." title="Merrill Lynch &amp; Co.">Merrill Lynch &amp; Co.</a> to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bank_of_America_Corp." title="Bank of America Corp.">Bank of America Corp.</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup></p>
<p>According to a 2009 BrandAsset Valuator survey taken of 17,000 people nationwide, the firm's reputation suffered in 2008 and 2009, and rival Morgan Stanley was respected more than Goldman Sachs, a reversal of the sentiment in 2006. Goldman refused to comment on the findings.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="TARP_and_Berkshire_Hathaway_investment_.282008.29">TARP and Berkshire Hathaway investment (2008)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Goldman_Sachs&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: TARP and Berkshire Hathaway investment (2008)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>On September 23, 2008, <a href="/wiki/Berkshire_Hathaway" title="Berkshire Hathaway">Berkshire Hathaway</a> agreed to purchase $5 billion in Goldman's preferred stock, and also received <a href="/wiki/Warrant_(finance)" title="Warrant (finance)">warrants</a> to buy another $5 billion in Goldman's <a href="/wiki/Common_stock" title="Common stock">common stock</a>, exercisable for a five-year term.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup> Goldman also received a $10 billion <a href="/wiki/Preferred_stock" title="Preferred stock">preferred stock</a> investment from the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/U.S._Treasury" title="U.S. Treasury">U.S. Treasury</a> in October 2008, as part of the <a href="/wiki/Troubled_Asset_Relief_Program" title="Troubled Asset Relief Program">Troubled Asset Relief Program</a> (TARP).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Andrew_Cuomo" title="Andrew Cuomo">Andrew Cuomo</a>, then <a href="/wiki/New_York_Attorney_General" title="New York Attorney General">Attorney General of New York</a>, questioned Goldman's decision to pay 953 employees bonuses of at least $1 million each after it received TARP funds in 2008.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup> That same period, however, CEO Lloyd Blankfein and six other senior executives opted to forgo bonuses, stating they believed it was the right thing to do, in light of "the fact that we are part of an industry that's directly associated with the ongoing economic distress".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup> Cuomo called the move "appropriate and prudent", and urged the executives of other banks to follow the firm's lead and refuse bonus payments.</p>
<p>In June 2009, Goldman Sachs repaid the U.S. Treasury’s TARP investment, with 23% interest (in the form of $318 million in <a href="/wiki/Preferred_stock" title="Preferred stock">preferred dividend</a> payments and $1.418 billion in warrant redemptions).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TARPrepay_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-TARPrepay-35">[35]</a></sup> On March 18, 2011, Goldman Sachs acquired <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Federal_Reserve" title="Federal Reserve">Federal Reserve</a> approval to buy back Berkshire's preferred stock in Goldman.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BerkshireHathwayPayback_36-0"><a href="#cite_note-BerkshireHathwayPayback-36">[36]</a></sup> In December 2009, Goldman announced their top 30 executives will be paid year-end bonuses in restricted stock, with <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/clawback" title="wikt:clawback">clawback</a> provisions, that must go unsold for five years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BBergGScomp_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-BBergGScomp-37">[37]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WSJbonus_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-WSJbonus-38">[38]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Use_of_Federal_Reserve.27s_Emergency_Liquidity_Programs_.282008.29">Use of Federal Reserve's Emergency Liquidity Programs (2008)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Goldman_Sachs&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Use of Federal Reserve's Emergency Liquidity Programs (2008)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>During the 2008 Financial Crisis, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Federal_Reserve" title="Federal Reserve">Federal Reserve</a> introduced a number of short-term credit and liquidity facilities to help stabilize markets. Some of the transactions under these facilities provided liquidity to institutions whose disorderly failure could have severely stressed an already fragile financial system.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup></p>
<p>Goldman Sachs was one of the heaviest users of these loan facilities, taking out numerous loans from March 18, 2008 – April 22, 2009. The <a href="/wiki/Primary_Dealer_Credit_Facility" title="Primary Dealer Credit Facility">Primary Dealer Credit Facility</a> (PDCF), the first Fed facility ever to provide overnight loans to investment banks, loaned Goldman Sachs a total of $589 billion against collateral such as corporate market instruments and <a href="/wiki/Mortgage-backed_security" title="Mortgage-backed security">mortgage-backed securities</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Term_Securities_Lending_Facility" title="Term Securities Lending Facility">Term Securities Lending Facility</a> (TSLF), which allows primary dealers to borrow liquid Treasury securities for one month in exchange for less liquid collateral, loaned Goldman Sachs a total of $193 billion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup></p>
<p>Goldman Sachs's borrowings totaled $782 billion in hundreds of transactions over these months.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup> This number is a total of all transactions over time and not the outstanding loan balance. The loans have been fully repaid in accordance with the terms of the facilities.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Apple_corporate_bond_sale_.282013.29">Apple corporate bond sale (2013)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Goldman_Sachs&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Apple corporate bond sale (2013)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In April 2013, Goldman Sachs Group Inc. together with <a href="/wiki/Deutsche_Bank" title="Deutsche Bank">Deutsche Bank</a> led <a href="/wiki/Apple_Inc." title="Apple Inc.">Apple's</a> largest corporate-bond deal in Apple Inc.'s history.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-The_Wall_Street_Journal_45-0"><a href="#cite_note-The_Wall_Street_Journal-45">[45]</a></sup> The $17 billion offering, which was the largest bond sale on record,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bloomberg_46-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bloomberg-46">[46]</a></sup> was Apple’s first since 1996. Goldman Sachs managed both of Apple’s previous bond offerings in the 1990s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bloomberg_46-1"><a href="#cite_note-Bloomberg-46">[46]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Underwriting_fees_received_from_Twitter_IPO_.282013.29">Underwriting fees received from Twitter IPO (2013)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Goldman_Sachs&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Underwriting fees received from Twitter IPO (2013)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Goldman Sachs was the lead underwriter (lead left) for <a href="/wiki/Twitter" title="Twitter">Twitter</a>’s initial public offering in 2013. At the time, Goldman’s position as lead underwriter was considered "one of the biggest tech prizes around".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup></p>
<p>On November 6, 2013, the day before Twitter was to be first traded on the NYSE, Goldman Sachs, as the lead left bank, issued 70 million shares of Twitter priced at $26 per share.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup> Twitter’s shares closed on the first day of trading at $44.90 per share, up 73%. This gave Twitter a company valuation of about $31 billion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup></p>
<p>According to regulatory filings the underwriting banks received 3.25% of the $1.82 billion raised by Twitter’s IPO. That came to approximately $59.2 million which was shared among the underwriters, which left lead bank, Goldman Sachs, receiving a larger, proportionate share. Of the 70 million shares offered on November 7 in Twitter’s IPO, Goldman Sachs was responsible for placing 27 million, entitling them to 39% of the entire fee pool, or about $22.8 million. Chief economist and strategist at ZT Wealth said, "Goldman being the first name on the S-1 has little to do with fees. This is about Goldman rebalancing itself as a serious leader and competing with Morgan Stanley’s dominant position in technology."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="SETLCoin_virtual_currency_.282014.29">SETLCoin virtual currency (2014)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Goldman_Sachs&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: SETLCoin virtual currency (2014)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>On October 30, 2014, Goldman Sachs filed a patent application for a virtual currency called SETLCoin.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Purchase_of_GE_Capital_Bank_.282015.29">Purchase of GE Capital Bank (2015)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Goldman_Sachs&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Purchase of GE Capital Bank (2015)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In August 2015, Goldman Sachs agreed to acquire <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/General_Electric_Co." title="General Electric Co.">General Electric Co.</a>'s GE Capital Bank on-line deposit platform. Terms of the transaction were not disclosed, but the purchase includes US$8-billion of on-line deposits and another US$8-billion of brokered certificates of deposit. The purchase allows Goldman Sachs to access a stable and inexpensive pool of source of funding.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup></p>
<p>In April 2016, Goldman Sachs launched an <a href="/wiki/Direct_bank" title="Direct bank">online-only savings bank</a>, GS Bank.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup> In October 2016, Goldman Sachs Bank USA started offering no-fee <a href="/wiki/Unsecured_debt" title="Unsecured debt">personal loans</a> under the brand <i>Marcus by Goldman Sachs</i>.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Purchase_of_Honest_Dollar_.282016.29">Purchase of Honest Dollar (2016)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Goldman_Sachs&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Purchase of Honest Dollar (2016)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In March 2016, Goldman Sachs agreed to acquire <a href="/wiki/Financial_technology" title="Financial technology">financial technology</a> startup <a href="/wiki/Whurley#Honest_Dollar_.282014-present.29" title="Whurley">Honest Dollar</a>, a digital retirement savings tool aimed at providing small-business employees and self-employed workers with affordable retirement plans. Terms of the deal were not disclosed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Corporate_affairs">Corporate affairs</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Goldman_Sachs&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Corporate affairs">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:30hudson.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1475" data-file-width="1117" height="291" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f0/30hudson.jpg/220px-30hudson.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f0/30hudson.jpg/330px-30hudson.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f0/30hudson.jpg/440px-30hudson.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>

<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:30hudson.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/30_Hudson_Street" title="30 Hudson Street">Goldman Sachs Tower</a>, at 30 Hudson Street, in Jersey City.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>As of 2013, Goldman Sachs employed 31,700 people worldwide.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BBergGScomp_37-1"><a href="#cite_note-BBergGScomp-37">[37]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Compensation">Compensation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Goldman_Sachs&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Compensation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>It was reported that the average total compensation per employee in 2006 was $622,000.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup> Also, the average compensation paid by Goldman, Sachs &amp; Co. to each employee in the first three months of 2013 was $135,594.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup> However, these numbers represent the <a href="/wiki/Arithmetic_mean" title="Arithmetic mean">arithmetic mean</a> of total compensation and is highly skewed upwards as several hundred of the top recipients command the majority of the Bonus Pools, leaving the median that most employees receive well below this number.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup></p>
<p>In Business Week's recent release of the <i>Best Places to Launch a Career 2008</i>, Goldman Sachs was ranked No.4 out of 119 total companies on the list.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup> The current Chief Executive Officer is Lloyd C. Blankfein. The company ranks No.1 in Annual <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Net_Income" title="Net Income">Net Income</a> when compared with 86 peers in the Investment Services sector. Blankfein received a $67.9 million bonus in his first year. He chose to receive "some" cash unlike his predecessor, Paulson, who chose to take his bonus entirely in company stock.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup></p>
<p>Investors have been complaining that the bank has near 11,000 more staffers than it did in 2005, but performances of workers were drastically in decline. In 2011, Goldman's 33,300 employees generated $28.8 billion in revenue and $2.5 billion in profit, but it represents a 25% decline in revenue per worker and a 71% decline in profit per worker compared with 2005. The staff cuts in its trading and investment banking divisions are possible as the company continues to reduce costs to raise profitability. In 2011, the company reduced its workforce by 2,400 positions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Litigation">Litigation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Goldman_Sachs&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Litigation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>On April 30, 2002, Goldman Sachs Group Inc was charged because extended the fraud-on-the-market doctrine of Basic Inc. v. Levinson, and one of analysts' misrepresentations affecting the market price of securities,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup> and paid $12,500,000 for settlement.</p>
<p>On July 15, 2003, Goldman Sachs &amp; Co had a lawsuit for artificially inflating the RSL's stock price by issuing untrue or materially misleading statements in research analyst reports, and paid $3,380,000 for settlement.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Current_operations">Current operations</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Goldman_Sachs&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Current operations">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:GoldmanSachsHeadquarters.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2048" data-file-width="1536" height="293" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c1/GoldmanSachsHeadquarters.JPG/220px-GoldmanSachsHeadquarters.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c1/GoldmanSachsHeadquarters.JPG/330px-GoldmanSachsHeadquarters.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c1/GoldmanSachsHeadquarters.JPG/440px-GoldmanSachsHeadquarters.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:GoldmanSachsHeadquarters.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Goldman_Sachs_Headquarters" title="Goldman Sachs Headquarters">Goldman Sachs Headquarters</a>, at 200 West Street, in Manhattan.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The company includes 4 businesses units, as follows:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-reutersoverview_64-0"><a href="#cite_note-reutersoverview-64">[64]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10K_65-0"><a href="#cite_note-10K-65">[65]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Investment_banking">Investment banking</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Goldman_Sachs&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Investment banking">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In 2015, <a href="/wiki/Investment_banking" title="Investment banking">investment banking</a> accounted for 21% of total company revenues.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10K_65-1"><a href="#cite_note-10K-65">[65]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Investment_banking" title="Investment banking">Investment banking</a> includes Financial Advisory (mergers and acquisitions, investitures, corporate defense activities, <a href="/wiki/Restructuring" title="Restructuring">restructuring</a> and spin-offs) and Underwriting (capital raises, public offerings, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Private_placements" title="Private placements">private placements</a> of equity and debt instruments).</p>
<p>Goldman Sachs is one of the leading M&amp;A advisory firms, often topping the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Thomson_Financial_league_tables" title="Thomson Financial league tables">Thomson Financial league tables</a> in sizes of transactions. The firm gained a reputation as a white knight in the mergers and acquisitions sector by advising clients on how to avoid unfriendly <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hostile_takeover" title="Hostile takeover">hostile takeovers</a>. For a long time during the 1980s, Goldman Sachs was the only major investment bank with a strict policy against helping to initiate a hostile takeover, which increased the firm's reputation immensely among sitting management teams at the time.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Sample_transaction:_Highway_privatization_.282007.29">Sample transaction: Highway privatization (2007)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Goldman_Sachs&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Sample transaction: Highway privatization (2007)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>The firm has been involved in brokering deals to privatize major highways by selling them to foreign investors, in addition to advising state and local governments – including Indiana, Texas, and Chicago – on privatization projects.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-The_Highwaymen_66-0"><a href="#cite_note-The_Highwaymen-66">[66]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Investing_.26_Lending">Investing &amp; Lending</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Goldman_Sachs&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Investing &amp; Lending">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In 2015, investing &amp; lending accounted for 16% of total company revenues.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10K_65-2"><a href="#cite_note-10K-65">[65]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Sample_investment:_Australian_loan_portfolio_.282013.29">Sample investment: Australian loan portfolio (2013)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Goldman_Sachs&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Sample investment: Australian loan portfolio (2013)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>In June 2013, Goldman Sachs's Special Situations Group, the proprietary investment unit of the investment bank, purchased a $863 million portion of Brisbane-based <a href="/wiki/Suncorp_Group" title="Suncorp Group">Suncorp Group Limited's</a> loan portfolio.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WSJ8aug2013_67-0"><a href="#cite_note-WSJ8aug2013-67">[67]</a></sup> The finance, insurance, and banking corporation is one of Australia's largest banks (by combined lending and deposits) and its largest <a href="/wiki/General_insurance" title="General insurance">general insurance</a> group.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GO_68-0"><a href="#cite_note-GO-68">[68]</a></sup> In 2013, <a href="/wiki/Distressed_securities" title="Distressed securities">distressed-debt investors</a>, seeking investment opportunities in the Asia-Pacific region, particularly in Australia, acquired discounted bonds or bank loans of companies facing distressed debt, with the potential of profitable returns if the companies' performance or their debt-linked assets improves. In 2013, Australia was one of the biggest markets for distressed-debt investors in the region.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WSJ8aug2013_67-1"><a href="#cite_note-WSJ8aug2013-67">[67]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Institutional_Client_Services">Institutional Client Services</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Goldman_Sachs&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Institutional Client Services">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In 2015, Institutional Client Services accounted for 45% of total company revenues.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10K_65-3"><a href="#cite_note-10K-65">[65]</a></sup></p>
<p>The segment is divided into four divisions and includes Fixed Income (the trading of interest rate and <a href="/wiki/Credit_(finance)" title="Credit (finance)">credit</a> products, <a href="/wiki/Mortgage-backed_security" title="Mortgage-backed security">mortgage-backed securities</a>, <a href="/wiki/Insurance-Linked_Securities_(ILS)" title="Insurance-Linked Securities (ILS)">insurance-linked securities</a> and structured and derivative products), Currency and Commodities (the trading of currencies and commodities), Equities (the trading of equities, <a href="/wiki/Equity_derivative" title="Equity derivative">equity derivatives</a>, <a href="/wiki/Structured_product" title="Structured product">structured products</a>, <a href="/wiki/Option_(finance)" title="Option (finance)">options</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Futures_contract" title="Futures contract">futures contracts</a>), and Principal Investments (merchant banking investments and funds). This segment consists of the revenues and profit gained from the Bank's trading activities, both on behalf of its clients (known as <a href="/wiki/Flow_trading" title="Flow trading">flow trading</a>) and for its own account (known as <a href="/wiki/Proprietary_trading" title="Proprietary trading">proprietary trading</a>).</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Investment_Management">Investment Management</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Goldman_Sachs&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Investment Management">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In 2015, investment management accounted for 18% of total company revenues.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10K_65-4"><a href="#cite_note-10K-65">[65]</a></sup></p>
<p>The Investment Management division provides investment advisory and financial planning services and offers investment products (primarily through <a href="/wiki/Separately_managed_account" title="Separately managed account">separately managed accounts</a> and commingled vehicles) across all major asset classes to a diverse group of institutions and individuals worldwide. The unit primarily generates revenues in the form of management and incentive fees. The division provides <a href="/wiki/Clearing_(finance)" title="Clearing (finance)">clearing</a>, financing, <a href="/wiki/Custodian_bank" title="Custodian bank">custody</a>, securities lending, and reporting services to institutional clients, including hedge funds, mutual funds, and pension funds. The division generates revenues primarily in the form of spreads, or management and transaction fees.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-reutersoverview_64-1"><a href="#cite_note-reutersoverview-64">[64]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 2009, the Goldman Sachs Asset Management hedge fund was the 9th largest in the United States, with $20.58 billion under management.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-alphamag_69-0"><a href="#cite_note-alphamag-69">[69]</a></sup> This was down from $32.5 billion in 2007, after client redemptions and weaker investment performance.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Shut_down_of_Global_Alpha_hedge_fund_.282011.29">Shut down of Global Alpha hedge fund (2011)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Goldman_Sachs&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Shut down of Global Alpha hedge fund (2011)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>On September 14, 2011, Goldman Sachs stated it was shutting down the Global Alpha fund, once the firm's largest hedge fund. The announcement followed a reported decline in fund balances to less than $1.7 billion in June 2011 from $11 billion in 2007. The decline was caused by investors withdrawing from the fund following earlier substantial market losses. The firm said it expected most of the fund assets to be liquidated by mid-October 2011.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Acquisition_of_business_from_Deutsche_Bank_.282013.29">Acquisition of business from Deutsche Bank (2013)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Goldman_Sachs&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Acquisition of business from Deutsche Bank (2013)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>In September 2013, Goldman Sachs Asset Management announced it had entered into an agreement with Deutsche Asset &amp; Wealth Management to acquire its stable value business, with total assets under supervision of $21.6 billion as of June 30, 2013.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="GS_Capital_Partners">GS Capital Partners</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Goldman_Sachs&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: GS Capital Partners">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Goldman_Sachs_Capital_Partners" title="Goldman Sachs Capital Partners">Goldman Sachs Capital Partners</a></div>
<p>GS Capital Partners is the private equity arm of Goldman Sachs that invests on behalf of institutional clients. It has invested over $17 billion in the 20 years from 1986 to 2006. One of the most prominent funds is the GS Capital Partners V fund, which comprises over $8.5 billion of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ownership_equity" title="Ownership equity">equity</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup> On April 23, 2007, Goldman closed GS Capital Partners VI with $20 billion in committed capital, $11 billion from qualified institutional and high-net-worth clients and $9 billion from the firm and its employees. GS Capital Partners VI is the current primary investment vehicle for Goldman Sachs to make large, privately negotiated equity investments.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup></p>
<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="Major_private_equity_assets">Major private equity assets</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Goldman_Sachs&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Major private equity assets">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h5>
<ul>
<li><a href="/wiki/Hongshi_Cement" title="Hongshi Cement">Hongshi Cement</a> (Cement)</li>
<li>The Ayco Company, L.P. (Financial Advisory)</li>
<li>Cogentrix Energy (Energy)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/American_Casino_%26_Entertainment_Properties" title="American Casino &amp; Entertainment Properties">American Casino &amp; Entertainment Properties</a> (Casinos)</li>
<li>CH James Restaurant Holdings (Quick Service Restaurant)</li>
<li>USI Holdings Corporation (Insurance &amp; Finance)</li>
<li>East Coast Power LLC (Energy)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Queens_Moat_Houses" title="Queens Moat Houses">Queens Moat Houses</a> (Hotels)</li>
<li>Sequoia Credit Consolidation (Finance)</li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Shineway_Industrial_Group" title="Shineway Industrial Group">Shineway Industrial Group</a> (Meat Processing)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Arcandor" title="Arcandor">Arcandor</a> <i>(former KarstadtQuelle property group – Retailer)</i></li>
<li>Medfinders Inc. (formerly <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nursefinders" title="Nursefinders">Nursefinders</a> Inc. – Healthcare)</li>
<li>Latin Force Group, LLC (Media)</li>
<li>Archon Hospitality Japan (Hotels)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/CMC_Markets" title="CMC Markets">CMC Markets</a> (Financial trading)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-timesCMC_76-0"><a href="#cite_note-timesCMC-76">[76]</a></sup></li>
</ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Predictions_regarding_emerging_markets_in_2005">Predictions regarding emerging markets in 2005</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Goldman_Sachs&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Predictions regarding emerging markets in 2005">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In December 2005, four years after its report on the emerging "<a href="/wiki/BRIC" title="BRIC">BRIC</a>" economies (Brazil, Russia, India, and China), Goldman Sachs named its "<a href="/wiki/Next_Eleven" title="Next Eleven">Next Eleven</a>"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup> list of countries, using macroeconomic stability, political maturity, openness of trade and investment policies and quality of education as criteria: <a href="/wiki/Bangladesh" title="Bangladesh">Bangladesh</a>, <a href="/wiki/Egypt" title="Egypt">Egypt</a>, <a href="/wiki/Indonesia" title="Indonesia">Indonesia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Iran" title="Iran">Iran</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mexico" title="Mexico">Mexico</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nigeria" title="Nigeria">Nigeria</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pakistan" title="Pakistan">Pakistan</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Philippines" title="Philippines">Philippines</a>, <a href="/wiki/Turkey" title="Turkey">Turkey</a>, <a href="/wiki/South_Korea" title="South Korea">South Korea</a> and <a href="/wiki/Vietnam" title="Vietnam">Vietnam</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[78]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Investment_banking_operations_in_Malaysia">Investment banking operations in Malaysia</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Goldman_Sachs&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Investment banking operations in Malaysia">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>According to <i><a href="/wiki/Thomson_Reuters" title="Thomson Reuters">Thomson Reuters</a></i> league table data, Goldman Sachs was the most successful foreign investment bank in Malaysia from 2011-2013. In 2013, the bank took a 21% market share in Malaysia's investment banking segment, double that of its nearest rival.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Corporate_citizenship">Corporate citizenship</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Goldman_Sachs&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: Corporate citizenship">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>During 2001-2009, the Goldman Sachs Global Leaders Program (GSGLP) identified 1,050 exceptional undergraduate students from 100 participating universities and colleges around the globe, awarded them "Global Leaders" in recognition of their academic excellence and leadership potential. Global Leaders study a diverse range of fields from economics to medical science. GSGLP alumni have gone on to win Rhodes, Truman, Marshall, Gates and Cambridge scholarships and Fulbright Fellowships.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup></p>
<p>Goldman Sachs has received favorable press coverage for conducting business and implementing internal policies related to reversing global climate change.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup> According to the company website, the Goldman Sachs Foundation has given $114 million in grants since 1999, with the goal of promoting youth education worldwide.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[82]</a></sup></p>
<p>The company also has been on <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fortune_Magazine" title="Fortune Magazine">Fortune Magazine</a>'s 100 Best Companies to Work For list since the list was launched in 1998, with emphasis placed on its support for employee philanthropic efforts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[83]</a></sup> The 2013 list cited the reason that the reported average annual compensation for an employee was more than $300,000.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[84]</a></sup> In November 2007, Goldman Sachs established a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Donor_advised_fund" title="Donor advised fund">donor advised fund</a> called Goldman Sachs Gives that donates to charitable organizations around the world, while increasing their maximum employee donation match to $20,000.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[85]</a></sup> The firm's Community TeamWorks is an annual, global volunteering initiative that in 2007 gave over 20,000 Goldman employees a day off work from May through August to volunteer in a team-based project organized with a local nonprofit organization.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gptwork_86-0"><a href="#cite_note-gptwork-86">[86]</a></sup></p>
<p>In March 2008, Goldman launched the <a href="/wiki/10,000_Women" title="10,000 Women">10,000 Women</a> initiative to train 10,000 women from predominantly developing countries in business and management.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gssmallbusiness_87-0"><a href="#cite_note-gssmallbusiness-87">[87]</a></sup></p>
<p>In November 2009, Goldman pledged $500 million to aid small businesses in their newly created <a href="/wiki/10,000_Small_Businesses" title="10,000 Small Businesses">10,000 Small Businesses</a> initiative. The initiative aims to provide 10,000 small businesses with assistance – ranging from business and management education and mentoring to lending and philanthropic support. The networking will be offered through partnerships with national and local business organizations, as well as employees of Goldman Sachs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1000businesses_88-0"><a href="#cite_note-1000businesses-88">[88]</a></sup> In addition to Goldman CEO Lloyd Blankfein, <a href="/wiki/Berkshire_Hathaway" title="Berkshire Hathaway">Berkshire Hathaway</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Warren_Buffett" title="Warren Buffett">Warren Buffett</a> and <a href="/wiki/Harvard_Business_School" title="Harvard Business School">Harvard Business School</a> professor <a href="/wiki/Michael_Porter" title="Michael Porter">Michael Porter</a> will chair the program's advisory council.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gssmallbusiness_87-1"><a href="#cite_note-gssmallbusiness-87">[87]</a></sup> Through the program, Goldman Sachs made a $2 million donation to <a href="/wiki/LaGuardia_Community_College" title="LaGuardia Community College">LaGuardia Community College</a> in New York.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[89]</a></sup></p>
<p>Goldman Sachs employees have been noted as highly loyal to their organization.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gptwork_86-1"><a href="#cite_note-gptwork-86">[86]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[90]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 2013, Goldman Sachs developed a "junior banker task force" of executives from around the world to improve analysts’ work environment and career development.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[91]</a></sup></p>
<p>Goldman underwrote and managed the Grand Parkway System Toll Revenue Bond for the Houston, Texas area in 2013. The bond was valued at $2.9 billion and was issued to finance the Grand Parkway System which will join suburban communities to major roads. The improved parkway system will insure that the Houston area will be able to accommodate what is the fastest growing metropolitan area in the country.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92"><a href="#cite_note-92">[92]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[93]</a></sup></p>
<p>Goldman invested $16 million to help build a 45,000 square-foot campus for 1,000 middle-school and high-school students in Lincoln Heights in Los Angeles for the Alliance College-Ready Public Schools charter system. The school opened in the fall of 2012.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94">[94]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95"><a href="#cite_note-95">[95]</a></sup></p>
<p>The CEO of the Global Impact Investing Network (GIIN), Luther Ragin, wrote in an article published in Investment Europe, that impact investing is meeting financial and philanthropic expectations, and that during 2014 Goldman Sachs sponsored a $250 million Social Impact Fund.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96">[96]</a></sup> Returns on the fund, which will be launched by the Urban Investment Group, are linked to the success of a variety of projects with high social value, including affordable housing projects, pre-school education, and an educational project for prisoners designed to reduce recidivism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[97]</a></sup> Between 2001 and 2014 over $3 billion of Goldman Sachs capital has been utilized for impact investing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[98]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Social_impact_bonds">Social impact bonds</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Goldman_Sachs&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: Social impact bonds">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In August 2012, Goldman Sachs created the first <a href="/wiki/Social_impact_bond" title="Social impact bond">social impact bond</a> in the United States. The "bond" is actually a $9.6 million loan to support the delivery of therapeutic services to 16- 18-year-olds incarcerated on <a href="/wiki/Rikers_Island" title="Rikers Island">Rikers Island</a>. The loan will be repaid based on the actual and projected cost savings realized by the <a href="/wiki/New_York_City_Department_of_Correction" title="New York City Department of Correction">New York City Department of Correction</a> as a result of the expected decrease in <a href="/wiki/Recidivism" title="Recidivism">recidivism</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99"><a href="#cite_note-99">[99]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[100]</a></sup></p>
<p>In June 2013, Goldman Sachs launched its second social impact bond. This bond is a loan of up to $4.6 million for a childhood education program in Salt Lake City, Utah. The United Way of Salt Lake said that the investment deal, by Goldman Sachs and J.B. Pritzker, could potentially benefit up to 3,700 children over multiple years and save state and local government millions of additional dollars.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101"><a href="#cite_note-101">[101]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102"><a href="#cite_note-102">[102]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[103]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Criticism_of_decline_in_tax_liability_in_2008">Criticism of decline in tax liability in 2008</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Goldman_Sachs&amp;action=edit&amp;section=34" title="Edit section: Criticism of decline in tax liability in 2008">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Goldman Sachs expected in December 2008 to pay $14 million in taxes worldwide for 2008 compared with $6 billion the previous year, after making $2.3 billion profit and paying $10.9 billion in employee pay and benefits. The company’s effective tax rate dropped to 1% from 34.1% in 2007, due to tax credits and, according to Goldman Sachs, "changes in geographic earnings mix" thus reducing the company's tax obligation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceB_104-0"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceB-104">[104]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105"><a href="#cite_note-105">[105]</a></sup> Many critics argue that the reduction in Goldman Sachs's tax rate was achieved by shifting its earnings to subsidiaries in low- or no-tax nations. Goldman Sachs had 28 such subsidiaries at the time, including 15 in the Cayman Islands.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106"><a href="#cite_note-106">[106]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Criticism_of_opposition_to_Basel_III">Criticism of opposition to Basel III</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Goldman_Sachs&amp;action=edit&amp;section=35" title="Edit section: Criticism of opposition to Basel III">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Basel_III" title="Basel III">Basel III</a></div>
<p>Large American and European banks, including Goldman Sachs, Morgan Stanley, and Deutsche Bank are part of the Washington D.C.-based <a href="/wiki/Institute_of_International_Finance" title="Institute of International Finance">Institute of International Finance</a>. They argued against <a href="/wiki/Basel_III" title="Basel III">Basel III</a> claiming it would hurt them and economic growth. The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/OECD" title="OECD">OECD</a> estimated that implementation of Basel III would decrease annual GDP growth by 0.05–0.15%,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Taylor2012_107-0"><a href="#cite_note-Taylor2012-107">[107]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-108"><a href="#cite_note-108">[108]</a></sup> blaming regulation as responsible for slow recovery from the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Late-2000s_financial_crisis" title="Late-2000s financial crisis">late-2000s financial crisis</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Taylor2012_107-1"><a href="#cite_note-Taylor2012-107">[107]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-109"><a href="#cite_note-109">[109]</a></sup> Basel III was also criticized as negatively affecting the stability of the financial system by increasing incentives of banks to game the regulatory framework.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-110"><a href="#cite_note-110">[110]</a></sup></p>
<p>The American Banker's Association,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SIFMA220ct2012_111-0"><a href="#cite_note-SIFMA220ct2012-111">[111]</a></sup> community banks organized in the Independent Community Bankers of America, and some of the most liberal Democrats in the U.S. Congress, including the entire Maryland congressional delegation with Democratic Sens. Cardin and Mikulski and Reps. Van Hollen and Cummings, voiced opposition to Basel III in their comments submitted to FDIC,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-112"><a href="#cite_note-112">[112]</a></sup> saying that the Basel III proposals, if implemented, would hurt small banks by increasing "their capital holdings dramatically on mortgage and small business loans."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-113"><a href="#cite_note-113">[113]</a></sup></p>
<p>Others have argued that Basel III did not go far enough to regulate banks as inadequate regulation was a cause of the financial crisis.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-114"><a href="#cite_note-114">[114]</a></sup> On January 6, 2013 the global banking sector won a significant easing of Basel III Rules, when the <a href="/wiki/Basel_Committee_on_Banking_Supervision" title="Basel Committee on Banking Supervision">Basel Committee on Banking Supervision</a> extended not only the implementation schedule to 2019, but broadened the definition of liquid assets.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-115"><a href="#cite_note-115">[115]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Controversies">Controversies</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Goldman_Sachs&amp;action=edit&amp;section=36" title="Edit section: Controversies">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Goldman has been harshly criticized, particularly in the aftermath of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/2007%E2%80%932012_global_financial_crisis" title="2007–2012 global financial crisis">2007–2012 global financial crisis</a>, where some alleged that it misled its investors and profited from the collapse of the mortgage market. That time — "one of the darkest chapters" in Goldman's history (according to the <i>New York Times</i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SORKIN_116-0"><a href="#cite_note-SORKIN-116">[116]</a></sup>) — brought investigations from the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Congress" title="United States Congress">Congress</a>, the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Department_of_Justice" title="United States Department of Justice">Justice Department</a>, and a lawsuit from the <a href="/wiki/U.S._Securities_and_Exchange_Commission" title="U.S. Securities and Exchange Commission">SEC</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FCIR-p192_117-0"><a href="#cite_note-FCIR-p192-117">[117]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-118"><a href="#cite_note-118">[118]</a></sup>—to whom it agreed to pay $550 million to settle.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-settle_119-0"><a href="#cite_note-settle-119">[119]</a></sup> It was "excoriated by the press and the public" (according to journalists McLean and Nocera<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-120"><a href="#cite_note-120">[120]</a></sup>) -- this despite the non-retail nature of its business that would normally have kept it out of the public eye.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Devils-361_121-0"><a href="#cite_note-Devils-361-121">[121]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kroll_122-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kroll-122">[122]</a></sup> Visibility and antagonism came from the $12.9 billion Goldman received—more than any other firm—from AIG counterparty payments provided by the New York Federal Reserve bailout; the $10 billion in <a href="/wiki/Troubled_Asset_Relief_Program" title="Troubled Asset Relief Program">TARP</a> money it received from the government (though the firm paid this back to the government);
