and a record $11.4 billion set aside for employee bonuses in the first half of 2009.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Devils-TARP_123-0"><a href="#cite_note-Devils-TARP-123">[123]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Harper_124-0"><a href="#cite_note-Harper-124">[124]</a></sup> While all the investment banks were scolded by congressional investigations, the company was subject to "a solo hearing in front of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Permanent_Subcommittee_on_Investigations#Wall_Street_and_the_Financial_Crisis" title="Permanent Subcommittee on Investigations">Senate Permanent Subcommitee on Investigations</a>" and a quite critical report.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Devils-361_121-1"><a href="#cite_note-Devils-361-121">[121]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-125"><a href="#cite_note-125">[125]</a></sup> In a widely publicized story,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-carney_126-0"><a href="#cite_note-carney-126">[126]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Matt_Taibbi" title="Matt Taibbi">Matt Taibbi</a> in <i><a href="/wiki/Rolling_Stone" title="Rolling Stone">Rolling Stone</a></i> characterized the firm as a "great vampire squid" sucking money instead of blood, allegedly engineering "every major market manipulation since the <a href="/wiki/Great_Depression" title="Great Depression">Great Depression</a> ... from tech stocks to high gas prices"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-carney_126-1"><a href="#cite_note-carney-126">[126]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Taibbi_127-0"><a href="#cite_note-Taibbi-127">[127]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Zamansky_128-0"><a href="#cite_note-Zamansky-128">[128]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-longlife_129-0"><a href="#cite_note-longlife-129">[129]</a></sup></p>
<p>Goldman Sachs has denied wrongdoing. It has stated that its customers were aware of its bets against the mortgage-related security products it was selling to them, and that it only used those bets to hedge against losses,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYTMorgenson23dec2009_130-0"><a href="#cite_note-NYTMorgenson23dec2009-130">[130]</a></sup> and was simply a <a href="/wiki/Market_maker" title="Market maker">market maker</a>. The firm also promised a "comprehensive examination of our business standards and practices", more disclosure and better relationships with clients.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-press_release_131-0"><a href="#cite_note-press_release-131">[131]</a></sup></p>
<p>Goldman has also been accused of an assortment of other misdeeds, varying from a general decline in ethical standards,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-topexec_132-0"><a href="#cite_note-topexec-132">[132]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-whyIam_133-0"><a href="#cite_note-whyIam-133">[133]</a></sup> working with dictatorial regimes,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GS_Russia_134-0"><a href="#cite_note-GS_Russia-134">[134]</a></sup> cozy relationships with the US federal government via a "<a href="/wiki/Revolving_door_(politics)" title="Revolving door (politics)">revolving door</a>" of former employees,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CRESWELL_135-0"><a href="#cite_note-CRESWELL-135">[135]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Insider_trading" title="Insider trading">insider trading</a> by some of its traders,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-james-npr_136-0"><a href="#cite_note-james-npr-136">[136]</a></sup> and driving up prices of <a href="/wiki/Commodity_market" title="Commodity market">commodities</a> through <a href="/wiki/Futures_exchange" title="Futures exchange">futures</a> speculation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-KOCIENIEWSKI_137-0"><a href="#cite_note-KOCIENIEWSKI-137">[137]</a></sup> Goldman has denied wrongdoing in these cases.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Sale_of_Dragon_Systems_to_Lernout_.26_Hauspie">Sale of Dragon Systems to Lernout &amp; Hauspie</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Goldman_Sachs&amp;action=edit&amp;section=37" title="Edit section: Sale of Dragon Systems to Lernout &amp; Hauspie">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In 2000, Goldman Sachs advised <a href="/wiki/Dragon_NaturallySpeaking" title="Dragon NaturallySpeaking">Dragon Systems</a> on its sale to the Belgian company, <a href="/wiki/Lernout_%26_Hauspie" title="Lernout &amp; Hauspie">Lernout &amp; Hauspie</a>. L&amp;H later collapsed due to accounting fraud. Jim and Janet Baker, founders and together 50% owners of Dragon, filed a lawsuit against Goldman Sachs, alleging that the firm did not warn Dragon or the Bakers of the accounting problems of the acquirer, and that this led to the loss of their portion of the sale price of $580 million, which was paid entirely in the form of the acquirer's stock. On January 23, 2013 a federal jury rejected the Bakers’ claims and found Goldman Sachs not liable to the Bakers for negligence, intentional and negligent misrepresentation, and breach of fiduciary duty.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-138"><a href="#cite_note-138">[138]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Involvement_in_the_European_sovereign_debt_crisis">Involvement in the European sovereign debt crisis</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Goldman_Sachs&amp;action=edit&amp;section=38" title="Edit section: Involvement in the European sovereign debt crisis">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Lucas_Papademos_2011-11-11.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="240" data-file-width="190" height="215" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/30/Lucas_Papademos_2011-11-11.jpg/170px-Lucas_Papademos_2011-11-11.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/3/30/Lucas_Papademos_2011-11-11.jpg 1.5x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Lucas_Papademos_2011-11-11.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Former Prime Minister of Greece Lucas Papademos</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Goldman is being criticized for its involvement in the 2010 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/European_sovereign_debt_crisis" title="European sovereign debt crisis">European sovereign debt crisis</a>. Goldman Sachs is reported to have systematically helped the Greek government mask the true facts concerning its national debt between the years 1998 and 2009.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-139"><a href="#cite_note-139">[139]</a></sup> In September 2009, Goldman Sachs, among others, created a special <a href="/wiki/Credit_default_swap" title="Credit default swap">credit default swap</a> (CDS) index to cover the high risk of Greece's national debt.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-140"><a href="#cite_note-140">[140]</a></sup> The interest-rates of Greek national bonds have soared to a very high level, leading the Greek economy very close to bankruptcy in March and May 2010 and again in June 2011.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-141"><a href="#cite_note-141">[141]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Lucas_Papademos" title="Lucas Papademos">Lucas Papademos</a>, Greece's former prime minister, ran the Central Bank of Greece at the time of the controversial derivatives deals with Goldman Sachs that enabled Greece to hide the size of its debt.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Foley_142-0"><a href="#cite_note-Foley-142">[142]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Petros_Christodoulou" title="Petros Christodoulou">Petros Christodoulou</a>, General Manager of the Greek <a href="/wiki/Public_Debt_Management_Agency_(Greece)" title="Public Debt Management Agency (Greece)">Public Debt Management Agency</a> is a former employee of Goldman Sachs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Foley_142-1"><a href="#cite_note-Foley-142">[142]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Mario_Monti" title="Mario Monti">Mario Monti</a>, Italy's former prime minister and finance minister, who headed the new government that took over after Berlusconi's resignation, is an international adviser to Goldman Sachs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Foley_142-2"><a href="#cite_note-Foley-142">[142]</a></sup> So is <a href="/wiki/Otmar_Issing" title="Otmar Issing">Otmar Issing</a>, former board member of the Bundesbank and the Executive Board of the European Bank.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Foley_142-3"><a href="#cite_note-Foley-142">[142]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Mario_Draghi" title="Mario Draghi">Mario Draghi</a>, the new head of the <a href="/wiki/European_Central_Bank" title="European Central Bank">European Central Bank</a>, is the former managing director of Goldman Sachs International.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Foley_142-4"><a href="#cite_note-Foley-142">[142]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ant%C3%B3nio_Borges_(economist)" title="António Borges (economist)">António Borges</a>, Head of the European Department of the <a href="/wiki/International_Monetary_Fund" title="International Monetary Fund">International Monetary Fund</a> in 2010–2011 and responsible for most of enterprise <a href="/wiki/Privatization" title="Privatization">privatizations</a> in <a href="/wiki/Portugal" title="Portugal">Portugal</a> since 2011, is the former Vice Chairman of Goldman Sachs International.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Foley_142-5"><a href="#cite_note-Foley-142">[142]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Carlos_Moedas" title="Carlos Moedas">Carlos Moedas</a>, a former Goldman Sachs employee, is the current Secretary of State to the <a href="/wiki/Prime_Minister_of_Portugal" title="Prime Minister of Portugal">Prime Minister of Portugal</a> and Director of ESAME, the agency created to monitor and control the implementation of the structural reforms agreed by the government of <a href="/wiki/Portugal" title="Portugal">Portugal</a> and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Troika_(triumvirate)" title="Troika (triumvirate)">troika</a> composed of the <a href="/wiki/European_Commission" title="European Commission">European Commission</a>, the European Central Bank and the International Monetary Fund. <a href="/wiki/Peter_Sutherland" title="Peter Sutherland">Peter Sutherland</a>, former Attorney General of Ireland is a non-executive director of Goldman Sachs International. These ties between Goldman Sachs and European leaders are an ongoing source of controversy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Foley_142-6"><a href="#cite_note-Foley-142">[142]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Employee.27s_Views">Employee's Views</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Goldman_Sachs&amp;action=edit&amp;section=39" title="Edit section: Employee's Views">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Greg_Smith_resignation_letter">Greg Smith resignation letter</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Goldman_Sachs&amp;action=edit&amp;section=40" title="Edit section: Greg Smith resignation letter">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>In his March 2012 resignation letter, printed as an <a href="/wiki/Op-ed" title="Op-ed">op-ed</a> in <i><a href="/wiki/The_New_York_Times" title="The New York Times">The New York Times</a></i>, the former head of Goldman Sachs US equity derivatives business in <a href="/wiki/Europe,_the_Middle_East_and_Africa" title="Europe, the Middle East and Africa">Europe, the Middle East and Africa</a> (EMEA) attacked the company's CEO and president for losing the company's culture, which he described as "the secret sauce that made this place great and allowed us to earn our clients' trust for 143 years". Smith said that advising clients "to do what I believe is right for them" was becoming increasingly unpopular. Instead there was a "toxic and destructive" environment in which "the interests of the client continue to be sidelined", senior management described clients as "<a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/muppet" title="wikt:muppet">muppets</a>" and colleagues callously talked about "ripping their clients off".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-topexec_132-1"><a href="#cite_note-topexec-132">[132]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-whyIam_133-1"><a href="#cite_note-whyIam-133">[133]</a></sup> In reply, Goldman Sachs said that "we will only be successful if our clients are successful", claiming "this fundamental truth lies at the heart of how we conduct ourselves" and that "we don't think [Smith's comments] reflect the way we run our business."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-143"><a href="#cite_note-143">[143]</a></sup> Later that year, Smith published a book titled <i>Why I left Goldman Sachs</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-144"><a href="#cite_note-144">[144]</a></sup></p>
<p>According to the <i>New York Times</i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">'</span>s own research after the op-ed was printed, almost all the claims made in Smith's incendiary Op-Ed–about Goldman Sachs's turned out to be "curiously short" on evidence. <i>The New York Times</i> never issued a retraction or admitted to any error in judgment in initially publishing Smith's op-ed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-145"><a href="#cite_note-145">[145]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="What_Happened_to_Goldman_Sachs_by_Steven_Mandis"><i>What Happened to Goldman Sachs</i> by Steven Mandis</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Goldman_Sachs&amp;action=edit&amp;section=41" title="Edit section: What Happened to Goldman Sachs by Steven Mandis">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Steven_Mandis_photo.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="640" data-file-width="427" height="255" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6d/Steven_Mandis_photo.jpg/170px-Steven_Mandis_photo.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6d/Steven_Mandis_photo.jpg/255px-Steven_Mandis_photo.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6d/Steven_Mandis_photo.jpg/340px-Steven_Mandis_photo.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Steven_Mandis_photo.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Steven Mandis</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In 2014 a book by former Goldman portfolio manager <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Steven_George_Mandis" title="Steven George Mandis">Steven George Mandis</a> was published entitled <i>What Happened to Goldman Sachs: An Insider's Story of Organizational Drift and Its Unintended Consequences</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HBR_146-0"><a href="#cite_note-HBR-146">[146]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-goodreads_147-0"><a href="#cite_note-goodreads-147">[147]</a></sup> Mandis also has a PhD dissertation about Goldman at <a href="/wiki/Columbia_University" title="Columbia University">Columbia University</a>. Mandis left in 2004 after working for the firm for 12 years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mandis_148-0"><a href="#cite_note-Mandis-148">[148]</a></sup> In an interview Mandis said, "You read about Goldman Sachs and it's either the bank is the best or the bank is the worst, this is not one of those books—things are never black or white." According to Mandis, there was an "organizational drift" in the company's evolution.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Cristina_Chen-Oster_and_Shanna_Orlich_Lawsuit">Cristina Chen-Oster and Shanna Orlich Lawsuit</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Goldman_Sachs&amp;action=edit&amp;section=42" title="Edit section: Cristina Chen-Oster and Shanna Orlich Lawsuit">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>In 2010, two former female employees filed a lawsuit against Goldman Sachs for gender discrimination. Cristina Chen-Oster and Shanna Orlich claimed that the firm fostered an "uncorrected culture of sexual harassment and assault" causing women to either be "sexualized or ignored". The suit cited both cultural and pay discrimination including frequent client trips to strip clubs, client golf outings that excluded female employees, and the fact that female vice presidents made 21% less than their male counterparts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-149"><a href="#cite_note-149">[149]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="California_bonds">California bonds</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Goldman_Sachs&amp;action=edit&amp;section=43" title="Edit section: California bonds">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>On November 11, 2008, the <i><a href="/wiki/Los_Angeles_Times" title="Los Angeles Times">Los Angeles Times</a></i> reported that Goldman Sachs had both earned $25 million from underwriting California bonds, and advised other clients to <a href="/wiki/Short_(finance)" title="Short (finance)">short</a> those bonds.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-150"><a href="#cite_note-150">[150]</a></sup> While some journalists criticized the contradictory actions,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-151"><a href="#cite_note-151">[151]</a></sup> others pointed out that the opposite investment decisions undertaken by the underwriting side and the trading side of the bank were normal and in line with regulations regarding <a href="/wiki/Chinese_wall#Finance" title="Chinese wall">Chinese walls</a>, and in fact critics had demanded increased independence between underwriting and trading.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-theatlantic_152-0"><a href="#cite_note-theatlantic-152">[152]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Personnel_.22revolving-door.22_with_US_government">Personnel "revolving-door" with US government</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Goldman_Sachs&amp;action=edit&amp;section=44" title='Edit section: Personnel "revolving-door" with US government'>edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>During 2008 Goldman Sachs received criticism for an apparent <a href="/wiki/Revolving_door_(politics)" title="Revolving door (politics)">revolving door</a> relationship, in which its employees and consultants have moved in and out of high level U.S. Government positions, creating the potential for conflicts of interest. The large number of former Goldman Sachs employees in the US government has been jokingly referred to "Government Sachs".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CRESWELL_135-1"><a href="#cite_note-CRESWELL-135">[135]</a></sup> Former Treasury Secretary Paulson is a former CEO of Goldman Sachs. Additional controversy attended the selection of former Goldman Sachs <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lobbyist" title="Lobbyist">lobbyist</a> <a href="/wiki/Mark_A._Patterson" title="Mark A. Patterson">Mark Patterson</a> as chief of staff to Treasury Secretary <a href="/wiki/Timothy_Geithner" title="Timothy Geithner">Timothy Geithner</a>, despite President <a href="/wiki/Barack_Obama" title="Barack Obama">Barack Obama</a>'s campaign promise that he would limit the influence of lobbyists in his administration.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-153"><a href="#cite_note-153">[153]</a></sup> In February 2011, the <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Washington_Examiner" title="Washington Examiner">Washington Examiner</a></i> reported that Goldman Sachs was "the company from which Obama raised the most money in 2008" and that its "CEO <a href="/wiki/Lloyd_Blankfein" title="Lloyd Blankfein">Lloyd Blankfein</a> has visited the White House 10 times."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-154"><a href="#cite_note-154">[154]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Former_New_York_Fed_Chairman.27s_ties_to_the_firm">Former New York Fed Chairman's ties to the firm</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Goldman_Sachs&amp;action=edit&amp;section=45" title="Edit section: Former New York Fed Chairman's ties to the firm">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p><a href="/wiki/Stephen_Friedman_(PFIAB)" title="Stephen Friedman (PFIAB)">Stephen Friedman</a>, a former director of Goldman Sachs, was named Chairman of the Federal Reserve Bank of New York in January 2008. Although he had retired from Goldman in 1994, Friedman continued to own stock in the firm. Goldman's conversion from a securities firm to a <a href="/wiki/Bank_holding_company" title="Bank holding company">bank holding company</a> in September 2008 meant it was now regulated by the Fed and not the SEC. When it became apparent that Timothy Geithner, then president of the New York Fed, would leave his role there to become Treasury Secretary, Friedman was granted a temporary one-year waiver of a rule that forbids "class C" directors of the Fed from direct interest with those it regulates. Friedman agreed to remain on the board until the end of 2009 to provide continuity in the wake of the turmoil caused by Lehman Brothers' bankruptcy. Had the waiver not been granted, the New York Fed would have lost both its president and its chairman (or Friedman would have had to divest his Goldman shares).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ok_155-0"><a href="#cite_note-ok-155">[155]</a></sup> This would have been highly disruptive for the New York Fed's role in the capital markets, and Friedman later said he agreed to stay on the NY Fed board out of a sense of public duty, but that his decision was "being mischaracterised as improper".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-156"><a href="#cite_note-156">[156]</a></sup></p>
<p>Media reports in May 2009 concerning Friedman's involvement with Goldman, and in particular, his purchase of the firm's stock when it traded at historical lows in the fourth quarter of 2008,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ok_155-1"><a href="#cite_note-ok-155">[155]</a></sup> fueled controversy and criticism over what was seen as a conflict of interest in Friedman's new role as supervisor and regulator to Goldman Sachs. These events prompted his resignation on May 7, 2009. Although Friedman's purchases of Goldman stock did not violate any Fed rule, statute, or policy, he said that the Fed did not need this distraction. He also stated his purchases, made while approval of a waiver was pending, were motivated by a desire to demonstrate confidence in the company during a time of market distress.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-157"><a href="#cite_note-157">[157]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Insider_trading_cases">Insider trading cases</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Goldman_Sachs&amp;action=edit&amp;section=46" title="Edit section: Insider trading cases">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In 1986, Goldman Sachs investment banker David Brown pleaded guilty to charges of passing inside information on a takeover deal that eventually was provided to <a href="/wiki/Ivan_Boesky" title="Ivan Boesky">Ivan Boesky</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-spreading_158-0"><a href="#cite_note-spreading-158">[158]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Robert_M._Freeman" title="Robert M. Freeman">Robert M. Freeman</a>, who was a senior Partner, who was the Head of Risk Arbitrage, and who was a protégé of <a href="/wiki/Robert_Rubin" title="Robert Rubin">Robert Rubin</a>, was also convicted of <a href="/wiki/Insider_trading" title="Insider trading">insider trading</a>, for his own account and for the firm's account.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-159"><a href="#cite_note-159">[159]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Rajat_Kumar_Gupta_-_World_Economic_Forum_Annual_Meeting_Davos_2010_crop.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3006" data-file-width="3200" height="207" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/83/Rajat_Kumar_Gupta_-_World_Economic_Forum_Annual_Meeting_Davos_2010_crop.jpg/220px-Rajat_Kumar_Gupta_-_World_Economic_Forum_Annual_Meeting_Davos_2010_crop.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/83/Rajat_Kumar_Gupta_-_World_Economic_Forum_Annual_Meeting_Davos_2010_crop.jpg/330px-Rajat_Kumar_Gupta_-_World_Economic_Forum_Annual_Meeting_Davos_2010_crop.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/83/Rajat_Kumar_Gupta_-_World_Economic_Forum_Annual_Meeting_Davos_2010_crop.jpg/440px-Rajat_Kumar_Gupta_-_World_Economic_Forum_Annual_Meeting_Davos_2010_crop.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Rajat_Kumar_Gupta_-_World_Economic_Forum_Annual_Meeting_Davos_2010_crop.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Rajat Gupta</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In April 2010, Goldman director <a href="/wiki/Rajat_Gupta" title="Rajat Gupta">Rajat Gupta</a> was named in an insider-trading case. It was said Gupta had "tipped off a hedge-fund billionaire", <a href="/wiki/Raj_Rajaratnam" title="Raj Rajaratnam">Raj Rajaratnam</a> of <a href="/wiki/Galleon_Group" title="Galleon Group">Galleon Group</a>, about the $5 billion Berkshire Hathaway investment in Goldman in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Financial_crisis_(2007%E2%80%93present)#Impacts_on_financial_institutions" title="Financial crisis (2007–present)">September, 2008</a>. According to the report, Gupta had told Goldman the month before his involvement became public that he wouldn't seek re-election as a director.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-james-npr_136-1"><a href="#cite_note-james-npr-136">[136]</a></sup> In early 2011, with the delayed Rajaratnam criminal trial about to begin,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-160"><a href="#cite_note-160">[160]</a></sup> the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/United_States_Securities_and_Exchange_Commission" title="United States Securities and Exchange Commission">United States Securities and Exchange Commission</a> (SEC) announced civil charges against Gupta covering the Berkshire investment as well as confidential quarterly earnings information from Goldman and <a href="/wiki/Procter_%26_Gamble" title="Procter &amp; Gamble">Procter &amp; Gamble</a> (P&amp;G). Gupta was board member at P&amp;G until voluntarily resigning the day of the SEC announcement, after the charges were announced. "Gupta was an investor in some of the Galleon hedge funds when he passed the information along, and he had other business interests with Rajaratnam that were potentially lucrative.... Rajaratnam used the information from Gupta to illegally profit in hedge fund trades.... The information on Goldman made Rajaratnam's funds $17 million richer.... The Procter &amp; Gamble data created illegal profits of more than $570,000 for Galleon funds managed by others, the SEC said." Gupta was said to have "vigorously denied the SEC accusations". He is also a board member of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/AMR_Corp." title="AMR Corp.">AMR Corp.</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-161"><a href="#cite_note-161">[161]</a></sup></p>
<p>Gupta was convicted in June 2012 on <a href="/wiki/Insider_trading" title="Insider trading">insider trading</a> charges stemming from <a href="/wiki/Raj_Rajaratnam/Galleon_Group,_Anil_Kumar,_and_Rajat_Gupta_insider_trading_cases" title="Raj Rajaratnam/Galleon Group, Anil Kumar, and Rajat Gupta insider trading cases">Galleon Group case</a> on <a href="#Insider_trading_conviction">four criminal felony counts</a> of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Conspiracy_(crime)" title="Conspiracy (crime)">conspiracy</a> and <a href="/wiki/Securities_fraud" title="Securities fraud">securities fraud</a>. He was sentenced in October 2012 to two years in prison, an additional year on supervised release and ordered to pay $5 million in fines.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-162"><a href="#cite_note-162">[162]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Stub_month_and_allegedly_misleading_results_in_December_2008">Stub month and allegedly misleading results in December 2008</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Goldman_Sachs&amp;action=edit&amp;section=47" title="Edit section: Stub month and allegedly misleading results in December 2008">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In April 2009, Goldman Sachs was accused of misleading investors by having "puffed up" its Q1 earnings by creating a December "orphan month" into which it shifted large writedowns, so they did not appear in any "quarterly number".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-deal_163-0"><a href="#cite_note-deal-163">[163]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-164"><a href="#cite_note-164">[164]</a></sup> In April 2009, the company reported a $780 million net loss for the single month of December alongside Q1 2009 (Jan–Mar) net earnings of $1.81 billion<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-deal_163-1"><a href="#cite_note-deal-163">[163]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-www2.goldmansachs.com_165-0"><a href="#cite_note-www2.goldmansachs.com-165">[165]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-off_166-0"><a href="#cite_note-off-166">[166]</a></sup></p>
<p>However, the accounting change to a calendar fiscal year, which created the stub month of December 2008, was required when the firm converted to a <a href="/wiki/Bank_holding_company" title="Bank holding company">bank holding company</a> and declared in Item 5.03 of its <a href="/wiki/Form_8-K" title="Form 8-K">Form 8-K</a> <a href="/wiki/U.S._Securities_and_Exchange_Commission" title="U.S. Securities and Exchange Commission">U.S. Securities and Exchange Commission</a> (SEC) filing of December 15, 2008.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-167"><a href="#cite_note-167">[167]</a></sup> The December loss also included a $850 million writedown on loans to bankrupt chemical maker <a href="/wiki/LyondellBasell" title="LyondellBasell">LyondellBasell</a>, as reported in late December (the chemical maker formally declared bankruptcy on January 6 but the loan would have been marked-to-market – it became clear in mid/late-December that Lyondell would not be able to meet its debt obligations).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-168"><a href="#cite_note-168">[168]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-169"><a href="#cite_note-169">[169]</a></sup></p>
<p>Most financial analysts and the mainstream financial press (<a href="/wiki/Bloomberg_L.P." title="Bloomberg L.P.">Bloomberg L.P.</a>, <a href="/wiki/Reuters" title="Reuters">Reuters</a>, etc.), aware of the accounting change and deteriorating market conditions into December, were not surprised by the December loss. Merrill Lynch took at least $8.1 billion of losses in the same period.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-170"><a href="#cite_note-170">[170]</a></sup> However, their lack of reaction and reporting of what was a widely expected result may have contributed to the surprise attributing this as a sign that the firm was trying to hide losses in December. On the contrary, the results of December 2008 were discussed up front and in detail by CFO <a href="/wiki/David_Viniar" title="David Viniar">David Viniar</a> in the first few minutes of the firm's Q1 2009 conference call, and were fully declared on page 10 of its earnings release document.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-www2.goldmansachs.com_165-1"><a href="#cite_note-www2.goldmansachs.com-165">[165]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-171"><a href="#cite_note-171">[171]</a></sup></p>
<p>On April 22, 2009, <a href="/wiki/Morgan_Stanley" title="Morgan Stanley">Morgan Stanley</a> reported a $1.3B net loss for the single month of December, alongside a $177M loss for the first quarter (Jan–Mar).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-172"><a href="#cite_note-172">[172]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-173"><a href="#cite_note-173">[173]</a></sup> However, whereas Goldman Sachs's first-quarter earnings (Jan–Mar) were well-above forecasts<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-174"><a href="#cite_note-174">[174]</a></sup> (which led to the speculation that the firm may have 'conveniently' shifted losses into December), Morgan Stanley's results for the same Jan–Mar period were below consensus estimates.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-175"><a href="#cite_note-175">[175]</a></sup> This, in addition to Morgan Stanley's losses in December, would appear to support Goldman's rejection of the notion that they deliberately shifted losses into December.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-176"><a href="#cite_note-176">[176]</a></sup> Like Goldman Sachs, Morgan Stanley converted to a <a href="/wiki/Bank_holding_company" title="Bank holding company">bank holding company</a> after the <a href="/wiki/Bankruptcy_of_Lehman_Brothers" title="Bankruptcy of Lehman Brothers">bankruptcy of Lehman Brothers</a> in September 2008.</p>
<p>In June 2009, the firm made some of the largest bonus payments in its history due to its strong financial performance.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WSJbonus_38-1"><a href="#cite_note-WSJbonus-38">[38]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-177"><a href="#cite_note-177">[177]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Involvement_with_the_bailout_of_AIG">Involvement with the bailout of AIG</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Goldman_Sachs&amp;action=edit&amp;section=48" title="Edit section: Involvement with the bailout of AIG">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/American_International_Group" title="American International Group">American International Group</a> (AIG) was bailed out by the US government in September 2008 after suffering a <a href="/wiki/Liquidity_crisis" title="Liquidity crisis">liquidity crisis</a>, whereby the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Federal_Reserve" title="Federal Reserve">Federal Reserve</a> initially lent $85 billion to AIG to allow the firm to meet its collateral and cash obligations.</p>
<p>In March 2009, it was reported that, in 2008, Goldman Sachs, (alongside other major US and international financial institutions), had received billions of dollars during the unwind of credit default swap (CDS) contracts purchased from AIG, including $12.9 billion from funds provided by the US <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Federal_Reserve" title="Federal Reserve">Federal Reserve</a> to bail out AIG.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-178"><a href="#cite_note-178">[178]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-179"><a href="#cite_note-179">[179]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-180"><a href="#cite_note-180">[180]</a></sup> (As of April, 2009, US Government loans to AIG totaled over $180 billion). The money was used to repay customers of its security-lending program and was paid as collateral to counterparties under credit insurance contracts purchased from AIG. However, due to the size and nature of the payouts there was considerable controversy in the media and amongst some politicians as to whether banks, including Goldman Sachs, may have benefited materially from the bailout and if they had been overpaid.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-deal_163-2"><a href="#cite_note-deal-163">[163]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-181"><a href="#cite_note-181">[181]</a></sup> The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/New_York_State_Attorney_General" title="New York State Attorney General">New York State Attorney General</a> <a href="/wiki/Andrew_Cuomo" title="Andrew Cuomo">Andrew Cuomo</a> announced in March 2009 that he was investigating whether AIG's trading counterparties improperly received government money.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-182"><a href="#cite_note-182">[182]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Firm.27s_response_to_criticism_of_AIG_payments">Firm's response to criticism of AIG payments</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Goldman_Sachs&amp;action=edit&amp;section=49" title="Edit section: Firm's response to criticism of AIG payments">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Goldman Sachs has maintained that its net exposure to AIG was 'not material', and that the firm was protected by <a href="/wiki/Hedge_(finance)" title="Hedge (finance)">hedges</a> (in the form of CDSs with other counterparties) and $7.5 billion of <a href="/wiki/Collateral_(finance)" title="Collateral (finance)">collateral</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nytimes1_183-0"><a href="#cite_note-nytimes1-183">[183]</a></sup> The firm stated the cost of these hedges to be over $100 million.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wk_184-0"><a href="#cite_note-wk-184">[184]</a></sup> According to Goldman, both the collateral and CDSs would have protected the bank from incurring an economic loss in the event of an AIG bankruptcy (however, because AIG was bailed out and not allowed to fail, these hedges did not pay out.)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-185"><a href="#cite_note-185">[185]</a></sup> CFO <a href="/wiki/David_Viniar" title="David Viniar">David Viniar</a> stated that profits related to AIG in Q1 2009 "rounded to zero", and profits in December were not significant. He went on to say that he was "mystified" by the interest the government and investors have shown in the bank's trading relationship with AIG.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-186"><a href="#cite_note-186">[186]</a></sup></p>
<p>Considerable speculation remains that Goldman's hedges against their AIG exposure would not have paid out if AIG was allowed to fail. According to a report by the United States Office of the Inspector General of <a href="/wiki/Troubled_Asset_Relief_Program" title="Troubled Asset Relief Program">TARP</a>, if AIG had collapsed, it would have made it difficult for Goldman to liquidate its trading positions with AIG, even at discounts, and it also would have put pressure on other counterparties that "might have made it difficult for Goldman Sachs to collect on the credit protection it had purchased against an AIG default."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sigtarp1_187-0"><a href="#cite_note-sigtarp1-187">[187]</a></sup> Finally, the report said, an AIG default would have forced Goldman Sachs to bear the risk of declines in the value of billions of dollars in collateral debt obligations.</p>
<p>Goldman argues that CDSs are <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mark-to-market" title="Mark-to-market">marked to market</a> (i.e. valued at their current market price) and their positions netted between counterparties daily. Thus, as the cost of insuring AIG's obligations against default rose substantially in the lead-up to its bailout, the sellers of the CDS contracts had to post more <a href="/wiki/Collateral_(finance)" title="Collateral (finance)">collateral</a> to Goldman Sachs. The firm claims this meant its hedges were effective and the firm would have been protected against an AIG bankruptcy and the risk of knock-on defaults, had AIG been allowed to fail.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wk_184-1"><a href="#cite_note-wk-184">[184]</a></sup> However, in practice, the collateral would not protect fully against losses both because protection sellers would not be required to post collateral that covered the complete loss during a bankruptcy and because the value of the collateral would be highly uncertain following the repercussions of an AIG bankruptcy.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (May 2010)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> As with the bankruptcy of <a href="/wiki/Lehman_Brothers" title="Lehman Brothers">Lehman Brothers</a>, wider and longer-term systemic and economic turmoil brought on by an AIG default would probably have affected the firm and all other market participants.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (May 2010)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Final_AIG_meetings_on_September_15_at_the_New_York_Federal_Reserve">Final AIG meetings on September 15 at the New York Federal Reserve</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Goldman_Sachs&amp;action=edit&amp;section=50" title="Edit section: Final AIG meetings on September 15 at the New York Federal Reserve">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Some have said, incorrectly according to others,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-188"><a href="#cite_note-188">[188]</a></sup> that Goldman Sachs received preferential treatment from the government by being the only Wall Street firm to have participated in the crucial September meetings at the New York Fed, which decided AIG's fate. Much of this has stemmed from an inaccurate but often quoted <i>New York Times</i> article.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-189"><a href="#cite_note-189">[189]</a></sup> The article was later corrected to state that Blankfein, CEO of Goldman Sachs, was "<i>one of</i> the Wall Street chief executives at the meeting" (emphasis added). <a href="/wiki/Bloomberg_L.P." title="Bloomberg L.P.">Bloomberg</a> has also reported that representatives from other firms were indeed present at the September AIG meetings.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-190"><a href="#cite_note-190">[190]</a></sup> Furthermore, Goldman Sachs CFO <a href="/wiki/David_Viniar" title="David Viniar">David Viniar</a> has stated that CEO Blankfein had never "met" with his predecessor and then-US Treasury Secretary Henry Paulson to discuss AIG;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-191"><a href="#cite_note-191">[191]</a></sup> However, there were frequent phone calls between the two of them.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-192"><a href="#cite_note-192">[192]</a></sup> Paulson was not present at the September meetings at the New York Fed. It is also a lesser known fact that <a href="/wiki/Morgan_Stanley" title="Morgan Stanley">Morgan Stanley</a> was hired by the Federal Reserve to advise them on the AIG bailout.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-193"><a href="#cite_note-193">[193]</a></sup></p>
<p>According to the <i>New York Times</i>, Paulson spoke with the CEO of Goldman Sachs two dozen times during the week of the bailout, though he obtained an ethics waiver before doing so.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYTethics_194-0"><a href="#cite_note-NYTethics-194">[194]</a></sup> While it is common for regulators to be in contact with market participants to gather valuable industry intelligence, particularly in a crisis, the <i>Times</i> noted he spoke with Goldman's Blankfein more frequently than with other large banks. Federal officials say that although Paulson was involved in decisions to rescue A.I.G, it was the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Federal_Reserve" title="Federal Reserve">Federal Reserve</a> that played the lead role in shaping and financing the A.I.G. bailout.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYTethics_194-1"><a href="#cite_note-NYTethics-194">[194]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id=".2460_million_settlement_for_Massachusetts_subprime_mortgages_.282009.29">$60 million settlement for Massachusetts subprime mortgages (2009)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Goldman_Sachs&amp;action=edit&amp;section=51" title="Edit section: $60 million settlement for Massachusetts subprime mortgages (2009)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>On May 10, 2009, the Goldman Sachs Group agreed to pay up to $60 million to end an investigation by the Massachusetts attorney general’s office into whether the firm helped promote unfair home loans in the state. The settlement will be used to reduce the mortgage payments of 714 Massachusetts residents who had secured subprime mortgages funded by Goldman Sachs. Michael DuVally, a spokesman for Goldman, said it was "pleased to have resolved this matter," and declined to comment further. This settlement may open the door to state government actions against Goldman throughout the United States aimed at securing compensation for predatory mortgage lending practices.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-195"><a href="#cite_note-195">[195]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Abacus_mortgage-backed_CDOs_.282010.29">Abacus mortgage-backed CDOs (2010)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Goldman_Sachs&amp;action=edit&amp;section=52" title="Edit section: Abacus mortgage-backed CDOs (2010)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Merrill_Lynch#CDO_controversies" title="Merrill Lynch">Merrill Lynch § CDO controversies</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Magnetar_Capital" title="Magnetar Capital">Magnetar Capital</a></div>
<p>In April 2010, the SEC charged Goldman Sachs and one of its vice presidents, <b>Fabrice Tourre</b>, with securities fraud. The SEC alleged that Goldman had told buyers of one of its investment deals (a type called a "synthetic CDO"), that the underlying assets in the deal had been picked by an independent CDO manager, ACA Management. In fact, a short investor betting that the CDO would default (<a href="/wiki/Paulson_%26_Co." title="Paulson &amp; Co.">Paulson &amp; Co.</a> hedge fund group) had played a "significant role" in the selection,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FCIR-p192_117-1"><a href="#cite_note-FCIR-p192-117">[117]</a></sup> and the package of securities turned out to become "one of the worst-performing mortgage deals of the housing crisis".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-asbad_196-0"><a href="#cite_note-asbad-196">[196]</a></sup> On July 15, 2010, Goldman settled, agreeing to pay the SEC and investors US$550 million.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-settle_119-1"><a href="#cite_note-settle-119">[119]</a></sup> In August 2013 Tourre was found liable on six of seven counts by a federal jury.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Raymond_197-0"><a href="#cite_note-Raymond-197">[197]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-liable_198-0"><a href="#cite_note-liable-198">[198]</a></sup></p>
<p>Unlike many investors and investment bankers, Goldman Sachs anticipated the <a href="/wiki/Subprime_mortgage_crisis" title="Subprime mortgage crisis">Subprime mortgage crisis</a> that developed in 2007-8.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYTMorgenson23dec2009_130-1"><a href="#cite_note-NYTMorgenson23dec2009-130">[130]</a></sup> Some of its traders became "bearish" on the housing boom beginning in 2004 and developed mortgage-related securities, originally intended to protect Goldman from investment losses in the housing market. In late 2006, Goldman management changed the firm's overall stance on the mortgage market, from positive to negative. As the market began its downturn, Goldman "created even more of these securities", no longer just hedging or satisfying investor orders but "enabling it to pocket huge profits" from the mortgage defaults, (according to business journalists Gretchen Morgenson, Bethany McLean and Joe Nocera).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYTMorgenson23dec2009-2_199-0"><a href="#cite_note-NYTMorgenson23dec2009-2-199">[199]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-200"><a href="#cite_note-200">[200]</a></sup></p>
<p>The complex securities were known as <a href="/wiki/Synthetic_CDO" title="Synthetic CDO">synthetic</a> CDOs (<a href="/wiki/Collateralized_debt_obligation" title="Collateralized debt obligation">collateralized debt obligations</a>). They were called synthetic because unlike regular CDOs, the principal and interest they paid out came not from mortgages or other loans, but from premiums to pay for insurance against mortgage defaults—the insurance known as "<a href="/wiki/Credit_default_swap" title="Credit default swap">credit default swaps</a>". Goldman and some other hedge funds held a "short" position with the securities, paying the premiums, while the investors (insurance companies, pension funds, etc.) receiving the premiums were the "long" position. The longs were responsible for paying the insurance "claim" to Goldman and any other shorts if the mortgages or other loans defaulted.</p>
<p>Through April 2007 Goldman issued over 20 CDOs in its "Abacus" series<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-201"><a href="#cite_note-201">[201]</a></sup> worth a total of US$10.9 billion. All together Goldman packaged, sold, and shorted a total of 47 synthetic CDOs, with an aggregate face value of US$66 billion between July 1, 2004, and May 31, 2007.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-202"><a href="#cite_note-202">[202]</a></sup></p>
<p>But while Goldman was praised for its foresight, some argued its bets against the securities it created gave it a vested interest in their failure. These securities performed very poorly (for the long investors) and by April 2010, Bloomberg reported that at least US$5 billion worth of the securities either carried "junk" ratings, or had defaulted.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-203"><a href="#cite_note-203">[203]</a></sup> One CDO examined by critics which Goldman bet against, but also sold to investors, was the US$800 million Hudson Mezzanine CDO issued in 2006. Goldman executives stated that the company was trying to remove subprime securities from its books in the Senate Permanent Subcommittee hearings. Unable to sell them directly it included them in the underlying securities of the CDO and took the short side, but critics (McLean and Nocera) complained the CDO prospectus did not explain this but described its contents as "'assets sourced from the Street', making it sound as though Goldman randomly selected the securities, instead of specifically creating a hedge for its own book."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-devils-p274-1_204-0"><a href="#cite_note-devils-p274-1-204">[204]</a></sup> The CDO did not perform well and by March 2008—just 18 months after its issue—so many borrowers had defaulted that holders of the security paid out "about US$310 million to Goldman and others who had bet against it".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYTMorgenson23dec2009_130-2"><a href="#cite_note-NYTMorgenson23dec2009-130">[130]</a></sup> Goldman's head of European fixed-income sales lamented in an email made public by the Senate Permanent Subcommittee on Investigations, the "real bad feeling across European sales about some of the trades we did with clients" who had invested in the CDO. "The damage this has done to our franchise is very significant."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-devils-p274-2_205-0"><a href="#cite_note-devils-p274-2-205">[205]</a></sup></p>
<p>Critics also complain that while Goldman's investors were large, ostensibly sophisticated banks and insurers, at least some of the CDO securities and their losses filtered down to small public agencies—"money used to run schools and fix potholes and fund municipal budgets"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-devils-ethics-1_206-0"><a href="#cite_note-devils-ethics-1-206">[206]</a></sup>—via debt sold by a structured investment vehicle of IKB bank, an ABACUS investor.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LATimes_207-0"><a href="#cite_note-LATimes-207">[207]</a></sup></p>
<p>In public statements Goldman claimed that it shorted simply to hedge and was not expecting the CDOs to fail. It also denied that its investors were unaware of Goldman's bets against the products it was selling to them.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYTMorgenson23dec2009_130-3"><a href="#cite_note-NYTMorgenson23dec2009-130">[130]</a></sup> This article describes the intricate links between Goldman Sachs trader, Jonathan M. Egol, synthetic collateralized debt obligations, or C.D.O., ABACUS, and asset-backed securities index (ABX).</p>
<p>Goldman is also alleged to have tried to pressure the credit rating service <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Moody%27s" title="Moody's">Moody's</a> to rate its products higher than the fundamentals called for.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYTMorgenson23dec2009_130-4"><a href="#cite_note-NYTMorgenson23dec2009-130">[130]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wang_208-0"><a href="#cite_note-Wang-208">[208]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="2010_SEC_civil_fraud_lawsuit">2010 SEC civil fraud lawsuit</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Goldman_Sachs&amp;action=edit&amp;section=53" title="Edit section: 2010 SEC civil fraud lawsuit">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>The particular synthetic CDO that the SEC's 2010 fraud suit charged Goldman with misleading investors with was called Abacus 2007-AC1. Unlike many of the Abacus securities, 2007-AC1 did not have Goldman Sachs as a short seller, in fact it lost money on the deal.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7pillars_209-0"><a href="#cite_note-7pillars-209">[209]</a></sup> That position was taken by the customer (<a href="/wiki/John_Paulson" title="John Paulson">John Paulson</a>) who hired Goldman to issue the security (according to the SEC's complaint). Paulson and his employees selected 90 BBB-rated mortgage bonds<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-asbad_196-1"><a href="#cite_note-asbad-196">[196]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-factbook_210-0"><a href="#cite_note-factbook-210">[210]</a></sup> that they believed were most likely to lose value and so the best bet to buy insurance for.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-settle_119-2"><a href="#cite_note-settle-119">[119]</a></sup> Paulson and the manager of the CDO, ACA Management, worked on the portfolio of 90 bonds to be insured (ACA allegedly unaware of Paulson's short position), coming to an agreement in late February 2007.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-factbook_210-1"><a href="#cite_note-factbook-210">[210]</a></sup> Paulson paid Goldman approximately US$15 million for its work in the deal.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-211"><a href="#cite_note-211">[211]</a></sup> Paulson ultimately made a US$1 billion from the short investments, the profits coming from the losses of the investors and their insurers. These were primarily <a href="/wiki/IKB_Deutsche_Industriebank" title="IKB Deutsche Industriebank">IKB Deutsche Industriebank</a> (US$150 million loss), and the investors and insurers of another US$900 million—ACA Financial Guaranty Corp,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mislead_212-0"><a href="#cite_note-mislead-212">[212]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ABN_AMRO_Bank_N.V." title="ABN AMRO Bank N.V.">ABN Amro</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Bank_of_Scotland" title="Royal Bank of Scotland">Royal Bank of Scotland</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sec21489_213-0"><a href="#cite_note-sec21489-213">[213]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mistake_214-0"><a href="#cite_note-Mistake-214">[214]</a></sup></p>
<p>The SEC alleged that Goldman "materially misstated and omitted facts in disclosure documents" about the financial security,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FCIR-p192_117-2"><a href="#cite_note-FCIR-p192-117">[117]</a></sup> including the fact that it had "permitted a client that was betting against the mortgage market [the hedge fund manager Paulson &amp; Co.] to heavily influence which mortgage securities to include in an investment portfolio, while telling other investors that the securities were selected by an independent, objective third party," ACA Management.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sec21489_213-1"><a href="#cite_note-sec21489-213">[213]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4-16-2010_215-0"><a href="#cite_note-4-16-2010-215">[215]</a></sup> The SEC further alleged that "Tourre also misled ACA into believing ... that Paulson's interests in the collateral section [sic] process were aligned with ACA's, when in reality Paulson's interests were sharply conflicting."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sec21489_213-2"><a href="#cite_note-sec21489-213">[213]</a></sup></p>
<p>In reply Goldman issued a statement saying the SEC's charges were "unfounded in law and fact", and in later statements maintained that it had not structured the portfolio to lose money,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Goldman-Media_Release_216-0"><a href="#cite_note-Goldman-Media_Release-216">[216]</a></sup> that it had provided extensive disclosure to the long investors in the CDO, that it had also lost money (US$90 million), that ACA selected the portfolio without Goldman suggesting Paulson was to be a long investor, that it did not disclose the identities of a buyer to a seller and vice versa as it was not normal business practice for a market maker,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Goldman-Media_Release_216-1"><a href="#cite_note-Goldman-Media_Release-216">[216]</a></sup> and that ACA was itself the largest purchaser of the Abacus pool, investing US$951 million. Goldman also stated that any investor losses resulted from the overall negative performance of the entire sector, rather than from a particular security in the CDO.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Goldman-Media_Release_216-2"><a href="#cite_note-Goldman-Media_Release-216">[216]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-217"><a href="#cite_note-217">[217]</a></sup></p>
<p>While some journalists and analysts have called these statements misleading,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-blogs.reuters.com_218-0"><a href="#cite_note-blogs.reuters.com-218">[218]</a></sup> others believed Goldman's defense was strong and the SEC's case was weak.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-219"><a href="#cite_note-219">[219]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-220"><a href="#cite_note-220">[220]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-221"><a href="#cite_note-221">[221]</a></sup></p>
<p>Some experts on securities law (such as <a href="/wiki/Duke_University_School_of_Law" title="Duke University School of Law">Duke University</a> law professor James Cox), believed the suit had merit because Goldman was aware of the relevance of Paulson's involvement and took steps to downplay it. Others, (including <a href="/wiki/Wayne_State_University_Law_School" title="Wayne State University Law School">Wayne State University</a> law professor Peter Henning), noted that the major purchasers were sophisticated investors capable of accurately assessing the risks involved, even without knowledge of the part played by Paulson.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-222"><a href="#cite_note-222">[222]</a></sup></p>
<p>Critics of Goldman Sachs point out that Paulson went to Goldman Sachs after being turned down for ethical reasons by another investment bank, <a href="/wiki/Bear_Stearns" title="Bear Stearns">Bear Stearns</a> who he had asked to build a CDO for him. Ira Wagner, the head of Bear Stearns’s CDO Group in 2007, told the <a href="/wiki/Financial_Crisis_Inquiry_Commission" title="Financial Crisis Inquiry Commission">Financial Crisis Inquiry Commission</a> that having the short investors select the referenced collateral as a serious conflict of interest and the structure of the deal Paulson was proposing encouraged Paulson to pick the worst assets.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-223"><a href="#cite_note-223">[223]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fiderer_224-0"><a href="#cite_note-Fiderer-224">[224]</a></sup> Describing Bear Stearns's reasoning, one author compared the deal to "a bettor asking a football owner to bench a star quarterback to improve the odds of his wager against the team."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Zuckerman_225-0"><a href="#cite_note-Zuckerman-225">[225]</a></sup> Goldman claimed it lost US$90 million, critics maintain it was simply unable (not due to a lack of trying) to shed its position before the underlying securities defaulted.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7pillars_209-1"><a href="#cite_note-7pillars-209">[209]</a></sup></p>
<p>Critics also question whether the deal was ethical, even if it was legal.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-devils-ethics-2_226-0"><a href="#cite_note-devils-ethics-2-226">[226]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-chron_227-0"><a href="#cite_note-chron-227">[227]</a></sup> Goldman had considerable advantages over its long customers. According to McLean and Nocera there were dozens of securities being insured in the CDO—for example, another ABACUS<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-228"><a href="#cite_note-228">[228]</a></sup>—had 130 credits from several different mortgage originators, commercial mortgage-backed securities, debt from Sallie Mae, credit cards, etc. Goldman bought mortgages to create securities, which made it "far more likely than its clients to have early knowledge" that the housing bubble was deflating and the mortgage originators like <a href="/wiki/New_Century" title="New Century">New Century</a> had begun to falsify documentation and sell mortgages to customers unable to pay the mortgage-holders back<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-McLean-272_229-0"><a href="#cite_note-McLean-272-229">[229]</a></sup>—which is why the fine print on at least one ABACUS prospectus warned long investors that the 'Protection Buyer' (Goldman) 'may have information, including material, non-public information' which it was not providing to the long investors.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-McLean-272_229-1"><a href="#cite_note-McLean-272-229">[229]</a></sup></p>
<p>Critics also worry about the attention drawn in Europe to the losses of European banks that might undermine the position of the US "as a safe harbor for the world's investors."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-chron_227-1"><a href="#cite_note-chron-227">[227]</a></sup></p>
<p>In the end, SEC suit did not go to court.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-econintersect_230-0"><a href="#cite_note-econintersect-230">[230]</a></sup> On July 15, 2010, three months after it filing, Goldman agreed to pay US$550 million—US$300 million to the U.S. government and US$250 million to investors, one of the largest penalties ever paid by a Wall Street firm.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-settle_119-3"><a href="#cite_note-settle-119">[119]</a></sup> The company did not admit or deny wrongdoing, but did admit that its marketing materials for the investment "contained incomplete information", and agreed to change some of its business practices regarding mortgage investments.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-settle_119-4"><a href="#cite_note-settle-119">[119]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_prosecutorial_actions">Other prosecutorial actions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Goldman_Sachs&amp;action=edit&amp;section=54" title="Edit section: Other prosecutorial actions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>On April 14, 2011, the United States Senate's Permanent Subcommittee on Investigations released a 635-page report entitled <i><a href="/wiki/Wall_Street_and_the_Financial_Crisis:_Anatomy_of_a_Financial_Collapse" title="Wall Street and the Financial Crisis: Anatomy of a Financial Collapse">Wall Street and the Financial Crisis: Anatomy of a Financial Collapse</a></i> which described some of the causes of the financial crisis. The report alleged that Goldman Sachs may have misled investors and profited from the collapse of the mortgage market at their expense.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-231"><a href="#cite_note-231">[231]</a></sup> The Chairman of the Subcommittee referred the report to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/United_States_Department_Of_Justice" title="United States Department Of Justice">U.S. Department of Justice</a> to determine whether Goldman executives had broken the law,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-232"><a href="#cite_note-232">[232]</a></sup> and two months later the Manhattan district attorney subpoenaed Goldman for relevant information on possible securities fraud,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SORKIN_116-1"><a href="#cite_note-SORKIN-116">[116]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Leonard_233-0"><a href="#cite_note-Leonard-233">[233]</a></sup> but on August 9 the Justice Department announced it had decided not to file charges against Goldman Sachs or its employees for trades made during the subprime mortgage portfolio.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-234"><a href="#cite_note-234">[234]</a></sup></p>
<p>The 2010 Goldman settlement did not cover charges against Goldman vice president and salesman for ABACUS,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7pillars_209-2"><a href="#cite_note-7pillars-209">[209]</a></sup> Fabrice Tourre.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-settle_119-5"><a href="#cite_note-settle-119">[119]</a></sup></p>
<p>Tourre unsuccessfully sought a dismissal of the suit,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-235"><a href="#cite_note-235">[235]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-236"><a href="#cite_note-236">[236]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-237"><a href="#cite_note-237">[237]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-238"><a href="#cite_note-238">[238]</a></sup> which then went to trial in 2013. On August 1, a federal jury found Tourre liable on six of seven counts, including that he misled investors about the mortgage deal. He was found not liable on the charge that he had deliberately made an untrue or misleading statement.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-liable_198-1"><a href="#cite_note-liable-198">[198]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Alleged_commodity_price_manipulation">Alleged commodity price manipulation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Goldman_Sachs&amp;action=edit&amp;section=55" title="Edit section: Alleged commodity price manipulation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>A provision of the 1999 financial deregulation law, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gramm-Leach-Bliley_Act" title="Gramm-Leach-Bliley Act">Gramm-Leach-Bliley Act</a>, allows commercial banks to enter into any business activity that is "complementary to a financial activity and does not pose a substantial risk to the safety or soundness of depository institutions or the financial system generally."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-devious_239-0"><a href="#cite_note-devious-239">[239]</a></sup> In the years since the laws passing, Goldman Sach and other investment banks (Morgan Stanley, JPMorgan Chase) have branched out into ownership of a wide variety of enterprises including raw materials, such as food products, zinc, copper, tin, nickel and, aluminum.</p>
<p>Some critics (such as Matt Taibbi) believe that allowing a company to both "control the supply of crucial physical commodities, and also trade in the financial products that might be related to those markets," is "akin to letting casino owners who take book on NFL games during the week also coach all the teams on Sundays."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-devious_239-1"><a href="#cite_note-devious-239">[239]</a></sup> <i>New York Times</i> journalist David Kocieniewski accused Goldman Sachs (and other Wall Street firms) of "capitalizing on loosened federal regulations" to manipulate "a variety of commodities markets", citing "financial records, regulatory documents and interviews with people involved in the activities." (The commodity highlighted by Kocieniewski and also mentioned by Taibbi was aluminum.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-KOCIENIEWSKI_137-1"><a href="#cite_note-KOCIENIEWSKI-137">[137]</a></sup>)</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Goldman_Sachs_Commodity_Index_and_the_2005.E2.80.932008_Food_Bubble">Goldman Sachs Commodity Index and the 2005–2008 Food Bubble</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Goldman_Sachs&amp;action=edit&amp;section=56" title="Edit section: Goldman Sachs Commodity Index and the 2005–2008 Food Bubble">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Goldman Sachs's creation of the <a href="/wiki/S%26P_GSCI" title="S&amp;P GSCI">Goldman Sachs Commodity Index</a> has been implicated by some in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/2007%E2%80%932008_world_food_price_crisis" title="2007–2008 world food price crisis">2007–2008 world food price crisis</a>.</p>
<p>In a 2010 article in <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Harper%27s_magazine" title="Harper's magazine">Harper's magazine</a></i>, <a href="/wiki/Frederick_Kaufman" title="Frederick Kaufman">Frederick Kaufman</a> argued that Goldman's creation of the Goldman Sachs Commodity Index<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-240"><a href="#cite_note-240">[240]</a></sup> helped passive investors (pension funds, mutual funds and others) enter the markets, through its own fund and through other commodity funds (sponsored by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/JPMorgan" title="JPMorgan">JPMorgan</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chase_(bank)" title="Chase (bank)">Chase</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/AIG" title="AIG">AIG</a>, etc.) following Goldman's lead. These funds disturbed the normal relationship between <a href="/wiki/Supply_and_demand" title="Supply and demand">supply and demand</a>, making prices more volatile and defeating the purpose of the exchanges (price stabilization) in the first place.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-241"><a href="#cite_note-241">[241]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-242"><a href="#cite_note-242">[242]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-243"><a href="#cite_note-243">[243]</a></sup></p>
<p>In a June 2010 article, <i><a href="/wiki/The_Economist" title="The Economist">The Economist</a></i> defended Goldman Sachs and other oil index-tracking funds citing a report by the <a href="/wiki/Organisation_for_Economic_Co-operation_and_Development" title="Organisation for Economic Co-operation and Development">Organisation for Economic Co-operation and Development</a> that found commodities without futures markets and ignored by index-tracking funds also saw price rises during the period.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-244"><a href="#cite_note-244">[244]</a></sup></p>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">See also <a href="/wiki/2000s_commodities_boom" title="2000s commodities boom">2000s commodities boom</a> for a discussion on specific causes of the <a href="/wiki/2000s_commodities_boom" title="2000s commodities boom">2000s commodities boom</a>.</div>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Aluminum_price_and_supply">Aluminum price and supply</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Goldman_Sachs&amp;action=edit&amp;section=57" title="Edit section: Aluminum price and supply">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>In August 2013, Goldman Sachs was subpoenaed by the federal <a href="/wiki/Commodity_Futures_Trading_Commission" title="Commodity Futures Trading Commission">Commodity Futures Trading Commission</a> as part of an investigation into complaints that Goldman-owned metals warehouses had "intentionally created delays and inflated the price of aluminum."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Inquiry_245-0"><a href="#cite_note-Inquiry-245">[245]</a></sup> In December 2013 it was announced that 26 cases accusing the Goldman Sachs Group Inc.—along with JPMorgan Chase &amp; Co., the two investment banks' warehousing businesses, and the London Metal Exchange in various combinations—of violating U.S. antitrust laws, would be assigned to <a href="/wiki/United_States_District_Court_for_the_Southern_District_of_New_York" title="United States District Court for the Southern District of New York">U.S. District</a> Judge <a href="/wiki/Katherine_B._Forrest" title="Katherine B. Forrest">Katherine B. Forrest</a> in <a href="/wiki/Manhattan" title="Manhattan">Manhattan</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Harris_246-0"><a href="#cite_note-Harris-246">[246]</a></sup></p>
<p>Following Goldman's purchase of the aluminum warehousing company Metro International<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-247"><a href="#cite_note-247">[247]</a></sup> in 2010, the wait of warehouse customers for delivery of aluminum supplies to their factories—to make beer cans, home siding and other products—went from an average of six weeks to more than 16 months, "according to industry records."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Zamansky_128-1"><a href="#cite_note-Zamansky-128">[128]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-KOCIENIEWSKI_137-2"><a href="#cite_note-KOCIENIEWSKI-137">[137]</a></sup></p>
<p>The cause of this was alleged to be Goldman's ownership of a quarter of the national supply of aluminum—a million and a half tons—in network of 27 Metro International warehouses Goldman owns in Detroit, Michigan.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-KOCIENIEWSKI_137-3"><a href="#cite_note-KOCIENIEWSKI-137">[137]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Stanley_248-0"><a href="#cite_note-Stanley-248">[248]</a></sup></p>
<p>"Aluminum industry analysts say that the lengthy delays at Metro International since Goldman took over are a major reason the premium on all aluminum sold in the spot market has doubled since 2010."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-KOCIENIEWSKI_137-4"><a href="#cite_note-KOCIENIEWSKI-137">[137]</a></sup> The price increase has cost "American consumers more than $5 billion"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-249"><a href="#cite_note-249">[249]</a></sup> according to former industry executives, analysts and consultants.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-KOCIENIEWSKI_137-5"><a href="#cite_note-KOCIENIEWSKI-137">[137]</a></sup></p>
<p>To avoid hoarding and price manipulation, the London Metal Exchange requires that "at least 3,000 tons of that metal must be moved out each day". Goldman has dealt with this requirement by moving the aluminum—not to factories, but "from one warehouse to another"—according to the Times.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-KOCIENIEWSKI_137-6"><a href="#cite_note-KOCIENIEWSKI-137">[137]</a></sup></p>
<p>According to Lydia DePillis of Wonkblog, when Goldman bought the warehouses it "started paying traders extra to bring their metal" to Goldman's warehouses "rather than anywhere else. The longer it stays, the more rent Goldman can charge, which is then passed on to the buyer in the form of a premium."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-250"><a href="#cite_note-250">[250]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DePillis_251-0"><a href="#cite_note-DePillis-251">[251]</a></sup> The effect is "amplified" by another company in the Netherlands (Glencore), which is "doing the same thing in its warehouse in Vlissingen".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DePillis_251-1"><a href="#cite_note-DePillis-251">[251]</a></sup></p>
<p>Michael DuVally, a spokesman for Goldman Sachs, has said the cases are without merit<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Harris_246-1"><a href="#cite_note-Harris-246">[246]</a></sup> and Robert Lenzner at <i>Forbes</i> says Goldman's control is only 3% of the global market and so too small to give it pricing power.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lenzner_252-0"><a href="#cite_note-Lenzner-252">[252]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Oil_futures_speculation">Oil futures speculation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Goldman_Sachs&amp;action=edit&amp;section=58" title="Edit section: Oil futures speculation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/2000s_commodities_boom" title="2000s commodities boom">2000s commodities boom</a></div>
<p>Investment banks, including Goldman, have also been accused of driving up the price of petrol/gasoline by speculating in the oil <a href="/wiki/Futures_exchange" title="Futures exchange">futures market</a>. In August 2011, "confidential documents" were leaked "detailing the positions"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sheppard_253-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sheppard-253">[253]</a></sup> in the oils futures market of several investment banks<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-254"><a href="#cite_note-254">[254]</a></sup>—including Goldman Sachs—on one day (June 30, 2008), just before the peak in high petrol/gasoline prices. The presence of positions by investment banks on the market was significant for the fact that the banks have deep pockets, and so the means to significantly sway prices, and unlike traditional market participants, neither produced oil nor ever took physical possession of actual barrels of oil they bought and sold. It was "a development that many say is artificially raising the price of crude" according to Kate Sheppard of Mother Jones.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sheppard_253-1"><a href="#cite_note-Sheppard-253">[253]</a></sup> However another source states "Just before crude oil hit its record high in mid-2008, 15 of the world’s largest banks were betting that prices would fall, according to private trading data...."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sanders_255-0"><a href="#cite_note-sanders-255">[255]</a></sup></p>
<p>In April 2011, a couple of observers—Brad Johnson of the blog <a href="/wiki/Joseph_J._Romm" title="Joseph J. Romm">Climate Progress</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-blame_256-0"><a href="#cite_note-blame-256">[256]</a></sup> and Alain Sherter of <a href="/wiki/CBS_MoneyWatch" title="CBS MoneyWatch">CBS News MoneyWatch</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sherter_257-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sherter-257">[257]</a></sup>—noted that Goldman Sachs was warning investors of a dangerous spike in the price of oil. Climate Progress quoted Goldman as warning "that the price of oil has grown out of control due to excessive speculation" in petroleum futures, and that "net speculative positions are four times as high as in June 2008." when the price of oil peaked.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-peaked_258-0"><a href="#cite_note-peaked-258">[258]</a></sup></p>
<p>It stated that "Goldman Sachs told its clients that it believed speculators like itself had artificially driven the price of oil at least $20 higher than supply and demand dictate."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-blame_256-1"><a href="#cite_note-blame-256">[256]</a></sup> Sherter noted that Goldman's concern over speculation did not prevent it (along with other speculators) from lobbying against regulations by the Commodity Futures Trading Commission to establish "position limits", which would cap the number of futures contracts a trader can hold, and thus prevent speculation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sherter_257-1"><a href="#cite_note-Sherter-257">[257]</a></sup></p>
