<p>According to Joseph P. Kennedy II, by 2012, prices on the oil commodity market had become influenced by "hedge funds and bankers" pumping "billions of purely speculative dollars into commodity exchanges, chasing a limited number of barrels and driving up the price".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-kennedy_259-0"><a href="#cite_note-kennedy-259">[259]</a></sup> The problem started, according to Kennedy, in 1991, when</p>
<blockquote>
<p>just a few years after oil futures began trading on the <a href="/wiki/New_York_Mercantile_Exchange" title="New York Mercantile Exchange">New York Mercantile Exchange</a>, Goldman Sachs made an argument to the Commodity Futures Trading Commission that Wall Street dealers who put down big bets on oil should be considered legitimate hedgers and granted an exemption from regulatory limits on their trades. The commission granted an exemption that ultimately allowed Goldman Sachs to process billions of dollars in speculative oil trades. Other exemptions followed<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-kennedy_259-1"><a href="#cite_note-kennedy-259">[259]</a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<p>and "by 2008, eight investment banks accounted for 32% of the total oil futures market."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-kennedy_259-2"><a href="#cite_note-kennedy-259">[259]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Initial_public_offering_kickback_bribes">Initial public offering kickback bribes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Goldman_Sachs&amp;action=edit&amp;section=59" title="Edit section: Initial public offering kickback bribes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Goldman Sachs is accused of asking for <a href="/wiki/Kickback_(bribery)" title="Kickback (bribery)">kickback bribes</a> from institutional clients who made large profits <a href="/wiki/Flipping" title="Flipping">flipping</a> stocks which Goldman had intentionally undervalued in <a href="/wiki/Initial_public_offering" title="Initial public offering">initial public offerings</a> it was underwriting. Documents <a href="/wiki/Under_seal" title="Under seal">under seal</a> in a decade-long lawsuit concerning <a href="/wiki/EToys.com" title="EToys.com">eToys.com</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Initial_public_offering" title="Initial public offering">initial public offering</a> (IPO) in 1999 but released accidentally to the <i>New York Times</i> show that IPOs managed by Goldman were underpriced and that Goldman asked clients able to profit from the prices to increase business with it. The clients willingly complied with these demands because they understood it was necessary in order to participate in further such undervalued IPOs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nocera_260-0"><a href="#cite_note-Nocera-260">[260]</a></sup> Companies going public and their initial consumer stockholders are both defrauded by this practice.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Salmon_261-0"><a href="#cite_note-Salmon-261">[261]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Taylor-related_civil_and_criminal_case">Taylor-related civil and criminal case</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Goldman_Sachs&amp;action=edit&amp;section=60" title="Edit section: Taylor-related civil and criminal case">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Former Goldman Sachs trader Matthew Marshall Taylor was convicted of hiding $8.3 billion worth of unauthorized trades involving derivatives on the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/S%26P_500" title="S&amp;P 500">S&amp;P 500</a> index by making "multiple false entries" into a Goldman trading system. When Goldman Sachs management uncovered the trades, Taylor was immediately fired. The trades cost the company $118 million, which Taylor was ordered to repay. In 2013, Taylor plead guilty to charges and was sentenced to 9 months in prison in addition to the monetary damages.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-262"><a href="#cite_note-262">[262]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Danish_utility_sale_.282014.29">Danish utility sale (2014)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Goldman_Sachs&amp;action=edit&amp;section=61" title="Edit section: Danish utility sale (2014)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Goldman Sachs's purchase of an 18% stake in state-owned <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dong_Energy" title="Dong Energy">Dong Energy</a>—<a href="/wiki/Denmark" title="Denmark">Denmark</a>'s largest electric utility—set off a "political crisis" in <a href="/wiki/Denmark" title="Denmark">Denmark</a>. The sale—approved in January 30, 2014—sparked protest in the form of the resignation of six cabinet ministers and the withdrawal of a party (<a href="/wiki/Socialist_People%27s_Party_(Denmark)" title="Socialist People's Party (Denmark)">Socialist People's Party</a>) from Prime Minister <a href="/wiki/Helle_Thorning-Schmidt" title="Helle Thorning-Schmidt">Helle Thorning-Schmidt</a>'s leftist governing coalition.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Levring_263-0"><a href="#cite_note-Levring-263">[263]</a></sup> According to <i><a href="/wiki/Bloomberg_Businessweek" title="Bloomberg Businessweek">Bloomberg Businessweek</a></i>, "the role of Goldman in the deal struck a nerve with the Danish public, which is still suffering from the aftereffects of the global financial crisis." Protesters in <a href="/wiki/Copenhagen" title="Copenhagen">Copenhagen</a> gathered around a banner "with a drawing of a vampire squid—the description of Goldman used by Matt Taibbi in Rolling Stone in 2009".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Levring_263-1"><a href="#cite_note-Levring-263">[263]</a></sup> Opponents expressed concern that Goldman would have some say in Dong's management, and that Goldman planned to manage its investment through "subsidiaries in Luxembourg, the Cayman Islands, and Delaware, which made Danes suspicious that the bank would shift earnings to tax havens."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Levring_263-2"><a href="#cite_note-Levring-263">[263]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Libya_investment_losses_.282013.29">Libya investment losses (2013)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Goldman_Sachs&amp;action=edit&amp;section=62" title="Edit section: Libya investment losses (2013)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In January 2014, the <a href="/wiki/Libyan_Investment_Authority" title="Libyan Investment Authority">Libyan Investment Authority</a> (LIA) filed a lawsuit against Goldman for $1 billion after the firm lost 98% of the $1.3 billion the LIA invested with Goldman in 2007.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hodges_264-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hodges-264">[264]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-forbes98_265-0"><a href="#cite_note-forbes98-265">[265]</a></sup> Goldman made more than $1 billion in derivatives trades with the LIA funds which lost almost all their value but earned Goldman $350 million in profit.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYT19082014_266-0"><a href="#cite_note-NYT19082014-266">[266]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-267"><a href="#cite_note-267">[267]</a></sup> In court documents firm has admitted to having used small gifts, occasional travel and an internship in order to gain access to Libya’s sovereign wealth fund.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-268"><a href="#cite_note-268">[268]</a></sup> In August 2014 Goldman dropped a bid to end the suit in a London court.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hodges_264-1"><a href="#cite_note-Hodges-264">[264]</a></sup> In October 2016, after trial, the court entered a judgment in Goldman Sachs's favor.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Improper_securities_lending_practices">Improper securities lending practices</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Goldman_Sachs&amp;action=edit&amp;section=63" title="Edit section: Improper securities lending practices">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In January 2016, Goldman Sachs agreed to pay $15 million after it was found that a team of Goldman employees, between 2008 and 2013, "granted locates" by arranging to borrow securities to settle short sales without adequate review. However, U.S. regulation for short selling requires brokerages to enter an agreement to borrow securities on behalf of customers or to have "reasonable grounds" for believing that it can borrow the security before entering contracts to complete the sale. Additionally, Goldman Sachs gave "incomplete and unclear" responses to information requests from SEC compliance examiners in 2013 about the firm's securities lending practices.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Improper_Securities_Lending_Practices_269-0"><a href="#cite_note-Improper_Securities_Lending_Practices-269">[269]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Malaysia_1MDB_scandal">Malaysia 1MDB scandal</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Goldman_Sachs&amp;action=edit&amp;section=64" title="Edit section: Malaysia 1MDB scandal">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>U.S. prosecutors are examining the role of Goldman Sachs in helping Malaysia's sovereign fund, 1MDB, to raise more than $6 billion. The 1MDB bond deals are said to generate "above-average" commission and fees for Goldman Sachs amounting close to $600m or more than 9 percent of the proceeds.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-270"><a href="#cite_note-270">[270]</a></sup></p>
<p>Goldman Sachs is reported by the Wall Street Journal to be under investigation for the $3 billion bond created by Goldman for 1MDB. Prosecutors are investigating if the bank failed to comply with the U.S. Bank Secrecy Act, which requires financial institutions to report suspicious transactions to regulators.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-271"><a href="#cite_note-271">[271]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Political_contributions">Political contributions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Goldman_Sachs&amp;action=edit&amp;section=65" title="Edit section: Political contributions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Goldman Sachs employees have donated to both major American political parties, as well as candidates and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Super_PAC" title="Super PAC">super PACs</a> belonging to both parties. According to the <a href="/wiki/Center_for_Responsive_Politics" title="Center for Responsive Politics">Center for Responsive Politics</a>, Goldman Sachs and its employees collectively gave $4.7 million in the <a href="/wiki/United_States_elections,_2014" title="United States elections, 2014">2014 elections</a> to various candidates, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Leadership_PAC" title="Leadership PAC">leadership PACs</a>, political parties, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/527_group" title="527 group">527 groups</a>, and outside spending entities.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-272"><a href="#cite_note-272">[272]</a></sup> In the 2016 election cycle, Goldman employees were reported (as of September 2016) to have donated $371,245 to the <a href="/wiki/Republican_National_Committee" title="Republican National Committee">Republican National Committee</a> and $301,119 to the <a href="/wiki/Hillary_Clinton_presidential_campaign,_2016" title="Hillary Clinton presidential campaign, 2016">Hillary Clinton presidential campaign</a>. Goldman Sachs forbade its top level employees from donating to Trump.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CNNDonationsPolicy_273-0"><a href="#cite_note-CNNDonationsPolicy-273">[273]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 2010, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Securities_and_Exchange_Commission" title="Securities and Exchange Commission">Securities and Exchange Commission</a> issued regulations that limit asset managers' donations to state and local officials, and prohibit certain top-level employees from donating to such officials.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CNNDonationsPolicy_273-1"><a href="#cite_note-CNNDonationsPolicy-273">[273]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MarketWatch_274-0"><a href="#cite_note-MarketWatch-274">[274]</a></sup> This SEC regulation is an anti-"pay-to-play" measure, intended to avoid the creation of a <a href="/wiki/Conflict_of_interest" title="Conflict of interest">conflict of interest</a>, or the appearance of a conflict of interest, as Goldman Sachs has business in managing state <a href="/wiki/Pension_fund" title="Pension fund">pension funds</a> and municipal debt.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CNNDonationsPolicy_273-2"><a href="#cite_note-CNNDonationsPolicy-273">[273]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MarketWatch_274-1"><a href="#cite_note-MarketWatch-274">[274]</a></sup> In 2016, Goldman Sachs's compliance department barred the firm's 450 partners (its most senior employees) from making donations to state or local officials as well as "any federal candidate who is a sitting state or local official."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CNNDonationsPolicy_273-3"><a href="#cite_note-CNNDonationsPolicy-273">[273]</a></sup> One effect of this rule is to bar Goldman partners from directly donating to <a href="/wiki/Donald_Trump" title="Donald Trump">Donald Trump</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Donald_Trump_presidential_campaign,_2016" title="Donald Trump presidential campaign, 2016">presidential campaign</a>, since Trump's vice presidential running mate, <a href="/wiki/Mike_Pence" title="Mike Pence">Mike Pence</a>, is the sitting <a href="/wiki/Governor_of_Indiana" title="Governor of Indiana">governor of Indiana</a>. Donations to <a href="/wiki/Hillary_Clinton" title="Hillary Clinton">Hillary Clinton</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Hillary_Clinton_presidential_campaign,_2016" title="Hillary Clinton presidential campaign, 2016">presidential campaign</a> are not barred by the policy, since neither Clinton nor her running mate <a href="/wiki/Tim_Kaine" title="Tim Kaine">Tim Kaine</a> are sitting state or local officials.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CNNDonationsPolicy_273-4"><a href="#cite_note-CNNDonationsPolicy-273">[273]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MarketWatch_274-2"><a href="#cite_note-MarketWatch-274">[274]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="List_of_officers_and_directors">List of officers and directors</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Goldman_Sachs&amp;action=edit&amp;section=66" title="Edit section: List of officers and directors">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Headquarters_and_other_major_offices">Headquarters and other major offices</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Goldman_Sachs&amp;action=edit&amp;section=67" title="Edit section: Headquarters and other major offices">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>In 1957 the head office moved to 20 <a href="/wiki/Broad_Street_(Manhattan)" title="Broad Street (Manhattan)">Broad Street</a>, New York.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cohan2012_11-13"><a href="#cite_note-Cohan2012-11">[11]</a></sup> Goldman Sachs's global headquarters is now located in New York City at <a href="/wiki/200_West_Street" title="200 West Street">200 West Street</a>. Its European headquarters are in <a href="/wiki/London" title="London">London</a> and its Asian headquarters are in <a href="/wiki/Singapore" title="Singapore">Singapore</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tokyo" title="Tokyo">Tokyo</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Hong_Kong" title="Hong Kong">Hong Kong</a>. Other major offices are in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jersey_City" title="Jersey City">Jersey City</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bangalore" title="Bangalore">Bangalore</a>, <a href="/wiki/Shanghai" title="Shanghai">Shanghai</a>, <a href="/wiki/Melbourne" title="Melbourne">Melbourne</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Salt_Lake_City" title="Salt Lake City">Salt Lake City</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2012-10K_277-0"><a href="#cite_note-2012-10K-277">[277]</a></sup></p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Goldman_Sachs_research_papers">Goldman Sachs research papers</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Goldman_Sachs&amp;action=edit&amp;section=68" title="Edit section: Goldman Sachs research papers">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Here is a list of notable Goldman Sachs research papers:</p>
<ul>
<li>Global Economics Paper No: 93 (South Africa Growth and Unemployment: A Ten-Year Outlook): Makes economic projections for <a href="/wiki/South_Africa" title="South Africa">South Africa</a> for the next 10 years. Published on May 13, 2003.</li>
<li>Global Economics Paper No: 66 (The World Needs Better Economic BRICs): Introduced the <a href="/wiki/BRIC" title="BRIC">BRIC</a> concept, which became highly popularized in the media and in economic research from this point on. Also made economic projections for 2050 for the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/G7" title="G7">G7</a> and South Africa as well. These were the first long-term economic projections which covered the GDP of numerous countries. Published on October 1, 2003.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-278"><a href="#cite_note-278">[278]</a></sup></li>
<li>Global Economics Paper No: 134 (How Solid are the BRICs): Introduced the <a href="/wiki/Next_Eleven" title="Next Eleven">Next Eleven</a> concept. Published on December 1, 2005.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-279"><a href="#cite_note-279">[279]</a></sup></li>
<li>Global Economics Paper No: 173 (New EU Member States—A Fifth BRIC?): Makes 2050 economic projections for the new <a href="/wiki/European_Union" title="European Union">EU</a> member states as a whole. Published on September 26, 2008.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-280"><a href="#cite_note-280">[280]</a></sup></li>
<li>Global Economics Paper No: 188 (A United Korea; Reassessing North Korea Risks (Part I)): Makes 2050 economic projections for North Korea in the hypothetical event that North Korea makes large free-market reforms right now. Published on September 21, 2009.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-281"><a href="#cite_note-281">[281]</a></sup></li>
<li>The Olympics and Economics 2012: Makes projections for the number of gold medals and told Olympic medals that each country wins at the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/2012_Olympics" title="2012 Olympics">2012 Olympics</a> using economic data and previous Olympic data. Published in 2012.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-282"><a href="#cite_note-282">[282]</a></sup></li>
</ul>

<li id="cite_note-asbad-196"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-asbad_196-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-asbad_196-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation news">Lucchetti, Aaron and; Serena Ng (April 20, 2010). <a class="external text" href="http://online.wsj.com/news/articles/SB10001424052748703757504575194521257607284" rel="nofollow">"Abacus Deal: As Bad as They Come"</a>. <i>Wall Street Journal</i><span class="reference-accessdate">. <li id="cite_note-NYTMorgenson23dec2009-2-199"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-NYTMorgenson23dec2009-2_199-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation news">Gretchen Morgenson; Louise Story (23 December 2009). <a class="external text" href="http://www.nytimes.com/2009/12/24/business/24trading.html?_r=4&amp;ref=business" rel="nofollow">"Banks Bundled Bad Debt, Bet Against It and Won"</a>. <i>New York Times</i>. <q>[Goldman] used the C.D.O.'s to place unusually large negative bets that were not mainly for hedging purposes</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AGoldman+Sachs&amp;rft.atitle=Banks+Bundled+Bad+Debt%2C+Bet+Against+It+and+Won&amp;rft.au=Gretchen+Morgenson&amp;rft.au=Louise+Story&amp;rft.date=2009-12-23&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fwww.nytimes.com%2F2009%2F12%2F24%2Fbusiness%2F24trading.html%3F_r%3D4%26ref%3Dbusiness&amp;rft.jtitle=New+York+Times&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal"><span style="display:none;"> </span></span></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-200"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-200">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation book">McLean and Nocera. <i>All the Devils Are Here</i>. p. 271. <q>But the firm's later insistence that it was merely a 'market maker' in these transactions—implying that it had no stake in the economic performance of the securities it was selling to clients—became less true over time.</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AGoldman+Sachs&amp;rft.au=McLean+and+Nocera&amp;rft.btitle=All+the+Devils+Are+Here&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.pages=271&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook"><span style="display:none;"> </span></span></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-201"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-201">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Bloomberg says 23: <a class="external text" href="http://www.bloomberg.com/news/2010-04-16/goldman-used-abacus-to-shuffle-debt-risk-like-beads.html" rel="nofollow">Abacus Let Goldman Shuffle Mortgage Risk Like Beads</a>. <i>New York Times</i> says 25 deals: <a class="external text" href="http://www.nytimes.com/2010/04/17/business/17goldman.html?pagewanted=all&amp;_r=0" rel="nofollow">S.E.C. <li id="cite_note-202"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-202">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><i>FCIR</i> p.145</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-203"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-203">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation news">Jody Shenn and Bob Ivry (April 16, 2010). <a class="external text" href="http://www.bloomberg.com/news/2010-04-16/goldman-used-abacus-to-shuffle-debt-risk-like-beads.html" rel="nofollow">"Abacus Let Goldman Shuffle Mortgage Risk Like Beads"</a>. Bloomberg<span class="reference-accessdate">. Retrieved <span class="nowrap">26 January</span> 2014</span>.</cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AGoldman+Sachs&amp;rft.atitle=Abacus+Let+Goldman+Shuffle+Mortgage+Risk+Like+Beads&amp;rft.date=2010-04-16&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fwww.bloomberg.com%2Fnews%2F2010-04-16%2Fgoldman-used-abacus-to-shuffle-debt-risk-like-beads.html&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal"><span style="display:none;"> </span></span> <span class="citation-comment" style="display:none; color:#33aa33">CS1 maint: Uses authors parameter (<a href="/wiki/Category:CS1_maint:_Uses_authors_parameter" title="Category:CS1 maint: Uses authors parameter">link</a>)</span></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-devils-p274-1-204"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-devils-p274-1_204-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation book">Bethany McLean; Joe Nocera. <i>All the Devils Are Here: The Hidden History of the Financial Crisis</i>. p. 274. <q>The CDO had been constructed, Goldman executives later told the Senate Permanent Subcommittee, while the company was trying to remove triple-B assets from its books. Among those assets was a long position in the ABX index that Goldman had gotten 'stuck' with while putting together deals for hedge fund clients that wanted to go short. Unable to find counterparties to take the long position off its hands, Goldman used Hudson as a means by which it hedged its long position. ... None of which was clear from the Hudson prospectus. Instead, the disclosure merely said that the CDO's contents were 'assets sourced from the Street,' making it sound as though Goldman randomly selected the securities, instead of specifically creating a hedge for its won book.</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AGoldman+Sachs&amp;rft.au=Bethany+McLean&amp;rft.au=Joe+Nocera&amp;rft.btitle=All+the+Devils+Are+Here%3A+The+Hidden+History+of+the+Financial+Crisis&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.pages=274&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook"><span style="display:none;"> </span></span></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-devils-p274-2-205"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-devils-p274-2_205-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation book">Bethany McLean; Joe Nocera. <i>All the Devils Are Here: The Hidden History of the Financial Crisis</i>. p. 274. <q>'Real bad feeling across European sales about some of the trades we did with clients' wrote Ysuyf Aliredha, Goldman's head of European fixed-income sales, in an e-mail to [Dan] Sparks [head of Goldman's Mortgage Desk]. 'The damage this has done to our franchise is very significant.'</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AGoldman+Sachs&amp;rft.au=Bethany+McLean&amp;rft.au=Joe+Nocera&amp;rft.btitle=All+the+Devils+Are+Here%3A+The+Hidden+History+of+the+Financial+Crisis&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.pages=274&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook"><span style="display:none;"> </span></span></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-devils-ethics-1-206"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-devils-ethics-1_206-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation book">Bethany McLean; Joe Nocera. <i>All the Devils Are Here: The Hidden History of the Financial Crisis</i>. pp. 280–1. <q>... IKB also created a structured investment vehicle called Rhinebridge. Rhinebridge, like other SIVs, issued debt that it then used to buy mortgage-backed securities and CDOs like Abacus. The debt issued by Rhinebridge, ... was bought by among others, King County, Washington, which managed money on behalf of one hundred other public agencies. This was money used to run schools and fix potholes and fund municipal budgets . ... For all of Goldman's later claims that it dealt only with the most sophisticated of investors, the fact remained that those investors could be fiduciaries, investing on behalf of school districts, fire departments, pensioners, and municipalities all across the country.</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AGoldman+Sachs&amp;rft.au=Bethany+McLean&amp;rft.au=Joe+Nocera&amp;rft.btitle=All+the+Devils+Are+Here%3A+The+Hidden+History+of+the+Financial+Crisis&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.pages=280-1&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook"><span style="display:none;"> </span></span></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-LATimes-207"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-LATimes_207-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation news">Nathaniel Popper (April 29, 2010). <a class="external text" href="http://articles.latimes.com/2010/apr/29/business/la-fi-goldman-impact-20100429" rel="nofollow">"Main St. paid for Wall St. maneuvers"</a>. <i>Los Angeles Times</i>. <q>a German bank known as IKB, paid for its share of the deal with money it collected from a number of relatively unsophisticated investors including King County in Washington state. In 2007, the county bought US$100 million of commercial paper, a type of short-term debt, from Rhineland, a special fund created by IKB that in turn snapped up nearly US$150 million of the securities created by the Goldman vehicle known as Abacus 2007-AC1. ... [King County also made a] US$50-million purchase ... in 2007 from another IKB fund, dubbed Rhinebridge. The county lost US$19 million when Rhinebridge collapsed—and an additional US$54 million when other similar funds defaulted. About 100 county agencies in the Seattle area, including some that deal with libraries and schools, saw their budgets cut as a result.</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AGoldman+Sachs&amp;rft.atitle=Main+St.+paid+for+Wall+St.+maneuvers&amp;rft.au=Nathaniel+Popper&amp;rft.date=2010-04-29&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Farticles.latimes.com%2F2010%2Fapr%2F29%2Fbusiness%2Fla-fi-goldman-impact-20100429&amp;rft.jtitle=Los+Angeles+Times&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal"><span style="display:none;"> </span></span></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-Wang-208"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-Wang_208-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation web">Wang, Marian (April 29, 2010). <a class="external text" href="http://www.propublica.org/blog/item/rating-agencies-banks-goldman-sachs-pressure" rel="nofollow">"Banks Pressured Credit Agencies, Then Blamed Them"</a>. ProPublica<span class="reference-accessdate">. Retrieved <span class="nowrap">18 February</span> 2014</span>. <q>Goldman and other firms often seemed to have pressured the agencies to give good ratings. E-mails released last week by the Senate investigations subcommittee give a glimpse of the back-and-forth (PDF). "I am getting serious pushback from Goldman on a deal that they want to go to market with today," wrote one Moody's employee in an internal e-mail message in April 2006. The Senate subcommittee found that rating decisions were often subject to concerns about losing market share to competitors. The agencies are, after all, paid by the firms whose products they rate.</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AGoldman+Sachs&amp;rft.aufirst=Marian&amp;rft.aulast=Wang&amp;rft.btitle=Banks+Pressured+Credit+Agencies%2C+Then+Blamed+Them&amp;rft.date=2010-04-29&amp;rft.genre=unknown&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fwww.propublica.org%2Fblog%2Fitem%2Frating-agencies-banks-goldman-sachs-pressure&amp;rft.pub=ProPublica&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook"><span style="display:none;"> </span></span></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-7pillars-209"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-7pillars_209-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-7pillars_209-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-7pillars_209-2"><sup><i><b>c</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation web">Whalen, Philip; Kara Tan Bhala. <a class="external text" href="http://sevenpillarsinstitute.org/case-studies/goldman-sachs-and-the-abacus-deal" rel="nofollow">"Goldman Sachs and The ABACUS Deal"</a>. Seven Pillars Institute for Global Finance and Ethics<span class="reference-accessdate">. Retrieved <span class="nowrap">9 February</span> 2014</span>.</cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AGoldman+Sachs&amp;rft.aufirst=Philip&amp;rft.au=Kara+Tan+Bhala&amp;rft.aulast=Whalen&amp;rft.btitle=Goldman+Sachs+and+The+ABACUS+Deal&amp;rft.genre=unknown&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fsevenpillarsinstitute.org%2Fcase-studies%2Fgoldman-sachs-and-the-abacus-deal&amp;rft.pub=Seven+Pillars+Institute+for+Global+Finance+and+Ethics&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook"><span style="display:none;"> </span></span></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-factbook-210"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-factbook_210-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-factbook_210-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation news">Wilchins, Dan (April 16, 2010). <a class="external text" href="http://www.reuters.com/article/2010/04/16/us-goldmansachs-abacus-factbox-idUSTRE63F5CZ20100416" rel="nofollow">"Factbox: How Goldman's ABACUS deal worked"</a>. <i>April 16, 2010</i>. Reuters<span class="reference-accessdate">. Retrieved <span class="nowrap">9 February</span> 2014</span>. <q>Hedge fund manager John Paulson tells Goldman Sachs in late 2006 he wants to bet against risky subprime mortgages using derivatives. The risky mortgage bonds that Paulson wanted to short were essentially subprime home loans that had been repackaged into bonds. The bonds were rated "BBB," meaning that as the home loans defaulted, these bonds would be among the first to feel the pain.</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AGoldman+Sachs&amp;rft.atitle=Factbox%3A+How+Goldman%27s+ABACUS+deal+worked&amp;rft.aufirst=Dan&amp;rft.aulast=Wilchins&amp;rft.date=2010-04-16&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fwww.reuters.com%2Farticle%2F2010%2F04%2F16%2Fus-goldmansachs-abacus-factbox-idUSTRE63F5CZ20100416&amp;rft.jtitle=April+16%2C+2010&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal"><span style="display:none;"> </span></span></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-211"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-211">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The $15 million has been described as "rent" for the Abacus name.<br/>
<cite class="citation book">Bethany McLean; Joe Nocera. <i>All the Devils Are Here: The Hidden History of the Financial Crisis</i>. p. 279. <q>Paulson knocked on Goldman's door at a fortuitous moment. The firm had begun thinking about 'ABACUS-renal strategies' ... By that, he meant that Goldman would 'rent'—for a hefty fee—the Abacus brand to a hedge fund that wanted to make a massive short bet. ... Paulson paid Goldman $15 million to rent the Abacus name.</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AGoldman+Sachs&amp;rft.au=Bethany+McLean&amp;rft.au=Joe+Nocera&amp;rft.btitle=All+the+Devils+Are+Here%3A+The+Hidden+History+of+the+Financial+Crisis&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.pages=279&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook"><span style="display:none;"> </span></span></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-mislead-212"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-mislead_212-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation news"><a class="external text" href="http://blogs.reuters.com/felix-salmon/2010/04/19/goldmans-misleading-statement-on-aca/" rel="nofollow">"Goldman's misleading statement on ACA"</a>. Reuters. April 19, 2010. <q>when Goldman wrapped the super-senior tranche of the Abacus deal, it did so with ABN Amro, a too-big-to-fail bank, and not with ACA. ABN Amro then laid off that risk onto ACA, but was on the hook for all of it if ACA went bust. As, of course, it did.</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AGoldman+Sachs&amp;rft.atitle=Goldman%27s+misleading+statement+on+ACA&amp;rft.date=2010-04-19&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fblogs.reuters.com%2Ffelix-salmon%2F2010%2F04%2F19%2Fgoldmans-misleading-statement-on-aca%2F&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal"><span style="display:none;"> </span></span></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-sec21489-213"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-sec21489_213-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-sec21489_213-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-sec21489_213-2"><sup><i><b>c</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation web"><a class="external text" href="http://www.sec.gov/litigation/complaints/2010/comp21489.pdf" rel="nofollow">"Securities and Exchange Commission vs Goldman Sachs &amp; Co &amp; Fabrice Tourre, Complaint (Securities Fraud)"</a> <span style="font-size:85%;">(PDF)</span>. <a href="/wiki/U.S._Securities_and_Exchange_Commission" title="U.S. Securities and Exchange Commission">U.S. Securities and Exchange Commission</a>. April 16, 2010<span class="reference-accessdate">. Retrieved <span class="nowrap">April 17,</span> 2010</span>.</cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AGoldman+Sachs&amp;rft.btitle=Securities+and+Exchange+Commission+vs+Goldman+Sachs+%26+Co+%26+Fabrice+Tourre%2C+Complaint+%28Securities+Fraud%29&amp;rft.date=2010-04-16&amp;rft.genre=unknown&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fwww.sec.gov%2Flitigation%2Fcomplaints%2F2010%2Fcomp21489.pdf&amp;rft.pub=U.S.+Securities+and+Exchange+Commission&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook"><span style="display:none;"> </span></span></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-Mistake-214"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-Mistake_214-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation news">THOMAS Jr., LANDON (April 22, 2010). <a class="external text" href="http://www.nytimes.com/2010/04/23/business/23cdo.html?_r=0" rel="nofollow">"A Routine Deal Became an $840 Million Mistake"</a>. <i>New York Times</i><span class="reference-accessdate">. Retrieved <span class="nowrap">11 February</span> 2014</span>. <q>R.B.S. [Royal Bank of Scotland] became involved in Abacus almost by accident. Bankers working in London for ABN Amro, a Dutch bank that was later acquired by R.B.S., agreed to stand behind a portfolio of American mortgage investments that were used in the deal. ABN Amro shouldered almost all of the risks for what, in retrospect, might seem like a small reward: that $7 million. When the housing market fell and Abacus collapsed, R.B.S. ended up on the hook for most of the losses.</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AGoldman+Sachs&amp;rft.atitle=A+Routine+Deal+Became+an+%24840+Million+Mistake&amp;rft.aufirst=LANDON&amp;rft.aulast=THOMAS+Jr.&amp;rft.date=2010-04-22&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fwww.nytimes.com%2F2010%2F04%2F23%2Fbusiness%2F23cdo.html%3F_r%3D0&amp;rft.jtitle=New+York+Times&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal"><span style="display:none;"> </span></span></span></li>

<li id="cite_note-Zuckerman-225"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-Zuckerman_225-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation web">Zuckerman, Gregory (April 19, 2010). <a class="external text" href="http://www.thedailybeast.com/articles/2010/04/19/inside-paulsons-deal-with-goldman.html" rel="nofollow">"Inside Paulson's Deal with Goldman"</a>. <i>Daily Beast</i><span class="reference-accessdate">. Retrieved <span class="nowrap">6 February</span> 2014</span>. <q>Scott Eichel, a senior Bear Stearns trader, was among those at the investment bank who sat through a meeting with Paulson but later turned down the idea. He worried that Paulson would want especially ugly mortgages for the CDOs, like a bettor asking a football owner to bench a star quarterback to improve the odds of his wager against the team. Either way, he felt it would look improper. ... it didn't pass the ethics standards; it was a reputation issue, and it didn't pass our moral compass.</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AGoldman+Sachs&amp;rft.atitle=Inside+Paulson%27s+Deal+with+Goldman&amp;rft.aufirst=Gregory&amp;rft.aulast=Zuckerman&amp;rft.date=2010-04-19&amp;rft.genre=unknown&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fwww.thedailybeast.com%2Farticles%2F2010%2F04%2F19%2Finside-paulsons-deal-with-goldman.html&amp;rft.jtitle=Daily+Beast&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal"><span style="display:none;"> </span></span></span></li>

<li id="cite_note-chron-227"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-chron_227-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-chron_227-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation web"><a class="external text" href="http://www.chron.com/opinion/editorials/article/The-Goldman-case-Legal-or-illegal-the-Abacus-1704276.php" rel="nofollow">"The Goldman case: Legal or illegal, the Abacus deal was morally wrong. Wall Street needs a new compass"</a>. <i>Houston Chronicle</i>. April 22, 2010<span class="reference-accessdate">. Retrieved <span class="nowrap">9 February</span> 2014</span>. <q>The involvement of European interests as losers in this allegedly fixed game has attracted the attention of that region's political leaders, most notably British Prime Minister Gordon Brown, who has accused Goldman of "moral bankruptcy". This is, in short, a big global story ... Is what Goldman Sachs did with its Abacus investment vehicle illegal? That will be for the courts to decide, ... But it doesn't take a judge and jury to conclude that, legalities aside, this was just wrong.</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AGoldman+Sachs&amp;rft.atitle=The+Goldman+case%3A+Legal+or+illegal%2C+the+Abacus+deal+was+morally+wrong.+Wall+Street+needs+a+new+compass&amp;rft.date=2010-04-22&amp;rft.genre=unknown&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fwww.chron.com%2Fopinion%2Feditorials%2Farticle%2FThe-Goldman-case-Legal-or-illegal-the-Abacus-1704276.php&amp;rft.jtitle=Houston+Chronicle&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal"><span style="display:none;"> </span></span></span></li>

The net positions of the banks undermine arguments made by Sanders that speculative trades on Wall Street drove oil prices in 2008, said Craig Pirrong, director of the Global Energy Management Institute at the University of Houston.
